Definition and Placement of the Chapter on Rights (Huquq) in Sales
Introduction defining rights as sale appendages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'huquq' in the context of Hanafi sales law and explain why the chapter on rights follows the chapter on riba, referencing the contrast between halal and haram excess.

Core Idea
Incorporeal rights attached to a sold item are legitimate benefits that follow the sale, contrasting with the forbidden excess in riba.

We have just completed the discussion of the principal elements of a sale — the sold item (mabi') and the price (thaman). Now the author turns to subsidiary rights (huquq) that accompany them. This placement is not arbitrary; it carries a deliberate juristic message: just as riba deals with an unlawful excess, this chapter deals with a lawful benefit arising from property rights. Understanding this connection helps you appreciate the structural logic of Hanafi fiqh texts.

Guided Path
1
Define the term 'huquq' as used in this chapter
Identify the exact definition given by the commentator: 'They are the established rights that follow the sold item and the price.' Note that 'huquq' here refers to physical appurtenances and customary benefits attached to the property, not abstract legal rights in a modern sense.
The term is plural of haqq, meaning a right or entitlement.
2
Understand the placement logic
The author delayed this chapter because rights are subsidiary (tawabi') to the principal object of sale. Just as you discuss the essential elements first, then the ancillary ones, the same order is followed in the text.
3
Connect to the preceding chapter on riba
Fath al-Qadir explains a thematic link: the riba chapter deals with haram excess (fadl haram), while this chapter deals with halal excess (fadl halal) that the buyer receives through attached rights. Recognize the contrast as a deliberate pedagogical device.
This highlights that not all benefits in a sale are prohibited; only those analogous to riba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحقوق وهي ما يتبع المبيع والثمن من الحقوق المعهودة (عيني)] لما فرغ من بيان ما هو أصل في البيع، وهو المبيع والثمن ذكر في هذا الباب ما يتبعهما من الحقوق المعهودة وله مناسبة خاصة بالربا؛ لأن بابه بیان فضل هو حرام، وهنا بيان فضل على المبيع هو حلال، وأخر الحقوق؛ لأنها توابع، فيليق ذكرها بعد مسائل البيوع. (فتح)
Chapter on rights (al-huquq): They are the established rights that follow the sold item and the price. (Al-`Ayni)] After finishing the explanation of what is principal in sale, i.e., the sold item and the price, he mentions in this chapter the established rights that follow them. It has a special relevance to riba because that chapter explains an excess (fadl) that is haram, while here it explains an excess on the sold item that is halal. He delayed [this chapter on] rights because they are subsidiary, so it is appropriate to mention them after the issues of sales. (Fath)
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This is the opening of the chapter on 'al-huquq' (rights), which refers to the ancillary rights that accompany the main object of sale (mabi') and the price (thaman). The commentator Al-`Ayni defines the term, and Fath al-Qadir explains the placement: after discussing the principal elements of sale, it is logical to discuss the subsidiary rights. Moreover, there is a thematic connection with riba: the riba chapter dealt with unlawful excess, while this chapter deals with lawful benefit that a purchaser obtains through the rights attached to the property.

Key Terms
حقوق (huquq): Ancillary rights that accompany the sold item, such as rights of passage, upper stories, etc.
المبيع (al-mabi'): The item being sold; the principal object of the sale contract.
الثمن (al-thaman): The price paid in exchange for the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the chapter on rights placed after the chapter on riba?
A) Because rights are more important than riba
B) Because rights are subsidiary and discuss a halal excess, contrasting with the haram excess in riba
C) Because riba invalidates rights, so they must come after
D) The passage does not give a reason

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, customary rights (huquq) attached to the sold item are automatically transferred unless excluded, and the chapter's placement after riba highlights the contrast between permissible and forbidden excess.

When Upper Story Enters the Sale of a Property
Ruling on inclusion of upper floor in sale of different property types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether the upper story is included in the sale of a bayt, manzil, and dar, and apply the required wording conditions for a manzil.

Core Idea
The upper floor is not automatically included; it depends on property type and wording.

Imagine you buy a property—does the upstairs come with it? In Hanafi law, the answer hinges on the specific term used: bayt, manzil, or dar. Each has its own default rule, and for a manzil, the buyer must use particular phrases to ensure inclusion. Local custom may also override these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property type
Determine whether the property sold is called a bayt (single chamber), manzil (dwelling with roofed courtyard), or dar (house with unroofed courtyard).
These terms have precise legal definitions in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Apply the default inclusion rule
For a bayt: the upper story is NOT included even if 'with every right' is said. For a dar: the upper story IS included automatically (like the latrine). For a manzil: inclusion depends on wording.
The exception: the zullah (shed) is not included in a dar.
3
Check the wording for manzil
For a manzil, the upper story enters only if the buyer says 'with every right belonging to it' (بكل حق هو له) or 'with its appurtenances' (بمرافقه) or 'with every little and much that is in it or from it' (بكل قليل وكثير هو فيه أو منه).
Mere general words like 'with everything' may not suffice.
4
Consider custom (urf)
Scholars note that these rules were based on Kufan custom. In other regions, local custom may determine that the upper floor enters all property types unless excluded.
Al-Kafi and later fatwa manuals allow following prevalent custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلو لا يدخلُ بشراء بيت بكلّ حق، وبشراء منزل إلا بكلّ حق هو له أو بمرافقه، أو بكل قليل وكثير هو فيه أو منه، ودخل بشراء دار كالكنيف لا الظُّلّة . أي أو يقول فحينئذ يدخل العلو العلو وإن لم يذكر شيئاً من ذلك
The upper floor does not enter by purchasing a house (bayt) with every right. And by purchasing a dwelling (manzil) [it does not enter] except with 'every right that belongs to it', or 'with its appurtenances', or 'with every little and much that is in it or from it'. And it enters by purchasing a home (dar), like the latrine, not the shed (zullah). That is, or if he says then the upper floor enters even if he does not mention anything of that.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage gives the rule for whether the upper story (علو - al-`uluww) of a building is included in the sale depending on the type of property and the wording used. If the property is called a 'bayt' (a house consisting of a single roofed chamber), the upper story is not included even if the buyer says 'with every right' because the upper story is a separate entity like a bayt. If it is a 'manzil' (a dwelling with rooms, a roofed courtyard, a kitchen, but no stable), the upper story is included only if the buyer explicitly mentions 'every right belonging to it' or 'with its appurtenances' (marafiq) or 'with every little and much that is in it or from it'. If it is a 'dar' (a house enclosed by walls with an unroofed courtyard), the upper story is automatically included without mention, just like the latrine (kanif) is included. However, the 'zullah' (a shed/veranda built over the public street) is not included in the sale of a dar according to Abu Hanifa, even if its door opens into the dar. The text also notes that in later custom ('urf), the upper story is included in all types if that is the prevalent custom.

Key Terms
بيت (bayt): A single roofed chamber suitable for sleeping; the upper story (علو) is considered a separate bayt.
منزل (manzil): A dwelling that includes multiple rooms, a roofed courtyard (صحة مسقف), and a kitchen, but no stable; it is similar to both a bayt and a dar.
دار (dar): A house enclosed by walls with an unroofed courtyard; it includes the upper story automatically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the upper floor (علو) enter the sale of a 'manzil'?
A) Automatically, like in a dar
B) Only if the buyer says 'with every right belonging to it' or similar phrases
C) It never enters
D) Only if the seller specifies it

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of the upper story depends on the property type (bayt/manzil/dar) and, for a manzil, on specific wording; the exception for zullah and the role of local custom must also be considered.

Inclusion of the Shed (Zullah) in the Sale of a House: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on whether the shed (zullah) is included in sale of a dar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on the inclusion of the zullah (shed) in the sale of a dar, explain the reasoning behind each position, and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
A structure built over the street is not included in a house sale according to Imam, but if its door is inside, Sahibayn include it as an appurtenance.

In Hanafi sales law, the default scope of a property sale includes its appurtenances (tawabi'). However, when a structure lies partly over public space, the jurists differed. This lesson examines the dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding the zullah — a shed built over the street — and how custom ultimately resolved the matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed element
What is the zullah and why is its inclusion contested? The zullah is a shed built over the public street but often attached to a house. The question is whether it automatically transfers with the sale of the house.
The latrine (kanif) is a clear example of an appurtenance that is always included.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa rules that the zullah is not included in the sale of the dar, regardless of where its door opens. His reasoning is that it is built over public airspace, thus it takes the ruling of the street.
For Abu Hanifa, explicit mention is required to include it.
3
State the position of Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that if the zullah's opening is inside the dar, it is included without explicit mention, as it is a functional appurtenance like the latrine or upper story.
The condition is that the door must open into the property.
4
Analyze the legal reasoning (ta‘lil)
Abu Hanifa emphasizes physical boundaries (built over public road); Sahibayn emphasize functional attachment (door inside dar). Both derive from qiyas: one analogizes to the road, the other to included appurtenances.
The difference is grounded in what is considered the primary determinant.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi scholars prefer the view of Sahibayn because prevailing custom treats such structures as part of the house. Therefore, the operative ruling for fatwa is that the zullah is included if its door opens into the dar.
This shows the role of custom (urf) in tarjih within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالكنيف: أي كما يدخل الكنيف - وهو بيت الخلاء – بشراء الدار لا الظلة. من غير إفراده بالذكر. (عيني) بضم كهيئة الصفة يقال لها بالفارسية ساباط أي لا تدخل الظلة في بيع الدار عند أبي حنيفة، سواء كان مفتحها في الدار أو لا، وعندهما تدخل بلا ذكر حقوق إن كان مفتحها في الدار؛ لأنها من توابع الدار كالكنيف والعلو وللإمام أنها خارجة عن الحدود مبنية على هواء الطريق، فأخذت حكمه، فلا تدخل إلا بذكر الحقوق ونحوها. (عيني، فتح)
Like the latrine: i.e., just as the latrine – which is the toilet – enters with the purchase of a dar, [the shed] does not. Without specifying it. (Al-`Ayni) With dammah [on the zay], like the form of a canopy; called 'sabat' in Persian. i.e., the shed does not enter in the sale of a dar according to Abu Hanifa, whether its opening is inside the dar or not. According to them (Sahibayn), it enters without mention of rights if its opening is in the dar, because it is among the appurtenances of the dar like the latrine and the upper story. For the Imam (Abu Hanifa), it is outside the boundaries, built over the air of the street, so it takes the ruling of the street; thus it does not enter except with mention of rights and the like. (Al-`Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling regarding the 'zullah' (a shed or veranda built over the public street, often called 'sabat' in Persian). In the sale of a dar, while the latrine (kanif) is automatically included, the zullah is not according to Abu Hanifa, regardless of whether its door opens into the dar. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the zullah is built over the public street, so it is outside the boundaries of the property and thus inherits the ruling of the street. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: if the opening of the zullah is within the dar, it is considered one of its appurtenances and is included without explicit mention, similar to the latrine and the upper story. The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Sahibayn, as custom supports including such structures if they are attached and accessible from the property.

Key Terms
ظلة (zullah): A shed or veranda built over the public street, often supported by columns, used as an entrance or shade.
كنيف (kanif): A latrine or toilet within the property; it is automatically included in the sale of a dar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition do Sahibayn say the zullah (shed) is included in the sale of a dar without explicit mention?
A) Always, regardless of opening
B) Never, only with explicit mention
C) If its opening is inside the dar
D) If it is mentioned in the contract

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the inclusion of a shed built over the street depends on whether its door opens into the property, with the later preferred view of Sahibayn considering it an appurtenance.

Inclusion of the Upper Floor in Bayt, Manzil, and Dar
Ruling on inclusion of upper floor in sale of three property types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether the upper floor is included when purchasing a bayt, manzil, or dar, and explain the linguistic and customary basis for each ruling.

Core Idea
The upper floor is never included in a bayt, conditionally in a manzil, and automatically in a dar.

In Hanafi property law, not all structures automatically transfer with a sale. Whether the upper floor (al-ulw) is included depends entirely on the type of property—bayt (single room), manzil (residence with some amenities), or dar (walled courtyard house). This lesson unpacks the default rules and the role of custom in overriding them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property type
Determine if the property is a bayt (single roofed dwelling), manzil (multiple rooms with roofed courtyard and kitchen but no stable), or dar (walled enclosure with unroofed courtyard and various rooms).
The text defines these terms precisely for legal purposes.
2
State the default inclusion rule
For bayt: ulw is NOT included even with the phrase 'bikulli haqq'. For manzil: ulw is NOT included unless the buyer uses specific phrases like 'bikulli haqq lahu' or 'bimarafiqihi'. For dar: ulw IS included automatically, like the latrine.
The rule for manzil is intermediate due to its resemblance to both bayt and dar.
3
Understand the linguistic reasoning
A bayt is a single roofed structure; the ulw is similar, so it cannot be subordinate. A dar encompasses everything within its walls, so ulw is part of it. A manzil shares features of both, so only explicit wording includes the ulw.
4
Apply the custom override
Al-Kafi notes these rules are based on Kufan custom. In other regions, the local custom is followed. In many places, the ulw is included in all property types regardless of nomenclature.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle of considering custom ('urf) in contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحقوق أي في بيان أحكامها العلو لا يدخلُ بشراء بيت بكلّ حق، وبشراء منزل إلا بكل حق هو له أو بمرافقه، أو بكل قليل وكثير هو فيه أو منه، ودخل بشراء دار كالكنيف لا الظُّلّة . أي أو يقول فحينئذ يدخل العلو العلو وإن لم يذكر شيئاً من ذلك باب الحقوق وهي ما يتبع المبيع والثمن من الحقوق المعهودة (عيني)] لما فرغ من بيان ما هو أصل في البيع، وهو المبيع والثمن ذكر في هذا الباب ما يتبعهما من الحقوق المعهودة وله مناسبة خاصة بالربا؛ لأن بابه بیان فضل هو حرام، وهنا بيان فضل على المبيع هو حلال، وأخر الحقوق؛ لأنها توابع، فيليق ذكرها بعد مسائل البيوع. (فتح) أجزائه، العلو: لأن البيت اسم لمسقف واحد يصلح للبيتوتة، والعلو مثله، والشيء لا يكون تبعاً لمثله، فلا يدخل بدون التنصيص عليه. وإن قال بكل حق" هو له"، وكذا لا يدخل بشراء منزل، إلا أن يقول المشتري: "بكل حق هو له"، أو يقول: "اشتريته بمرافقه"، أو يقول : اشتريته" بكل قليل أو كثير هو في المنزل أو من المنزل"؛ لأن المنزل له شبه بالدار، وشبه بالبيت؛ لأنه اسم لما يشتمل على بيوت وصحن مسقف ومطبخ يسكن فيه الرجل بأهله مع ضرب قصور فيه؛ لأنه ليس فيه إصطبل. فلشبهه بالدار يدخل العلو تبعاً عند ذكر التوابع مثل أن يقول: بكل حق هو له، أو نحو ذلك، ولشبهه بالبيت لا يدخل العلو في المنزل بدون ذكر التوابع، ويدخل بشراء دار وإن لم يذكر شيئاً ذلك؛ لأن الدار من اسم لما أدير عليه الحدود من الحائط ويشتمل على بيوت ومنازل وصحن غير مسقف، والعلو من فيدخل فيه من غير ذكر، كما يدخل الكنيف بشراء الدار من غير إفراده بالذكر، وكذا يدخل بئر الماء والأشجار التي في صحنها والبستان فيها، وفي "الكافي": هذا التفصيل بناء على عرف أهل الكوفة، وفي عرفنا يدخل العلو في الكل، سواء باع باسم البيت أو المنزل أو الدار؛ لأن كل مسكن كان صغيراً أو كبيراً، خانة، سواء يسمى فيعتبر في كل إقليم، وفي كل عصر عرف أهله. رمز الحقائق: ٦٢/٢، ٦٣] (فتح) بكل حق أي لو اشترى بيتا فوقه بيت لا يدخل العلو وإن قال : اشتريت بكل حق إلا أن ينص عليه. (مسكين) وبشراء: أي ولا يدخل العلو بشراء إلخ كالكنيف: أي كما يدخل الكنيف - وهو بيت الخلاء – بشراء الدار لا الظلة. من غير إفراده بالذكر. (عيني) بضم كهيئة الصفة يقال لها بالفارسية ساباط أي لا تدخل الظلة في بيع الدار عند أبي حنيفة، سواء كان مفتحها في الدار أو لا، وعندهما تدخل بلا ذكر حقوق إن كان مفتحها في الدار؛ لأنها من توابع الدار كالكنيف والعلو وللإمام أنها خارجة عن الحدود مبنية على هواء الطريق، فأخذت حكمه، فلا تدخل إلا بذكر الحقوق ونحوها. (عيني، فتح)
Chapter of Rights: i.e. explaining their rulings. The upper floor is not included in the purchase of a house (bayt) even with 'with every right', nor in the purchase of a residence (manzil) except with 'every right belonging to it' or 'with its appurtenances' or 'with every small and great thing in it or from it'. And it is included in the purchase of a courtyard house (dar) like the latrine, not the sunshade. That is, if he says... then the upper floor is included even if he does not mention any of that. Chapter of Rights: they are what follows from the sold item and the price of the customary rights. When he finished explaining what is the basis in sale, which is the sold item and the price, he mentioned in this chapter what follows them of the customary rights. And it has a special connection to riba because its chapter is explaining an excess that is haram, and here is an explanation of an excess on the sold item that is halal. And he delayed rights because they are ancillary, so it is appropriate to mention them after the issues of sales. (Fath) Its parts: The upper floor because 'house' is a noun for a single roofed structure suitable for dwelling, and the upper floor is similar to it, and a thing is not a subordinate to its like, so it is not included without explicit textual mention. If he says 'with every right belonging to it', likewise it is not included in the purchase of a residence, unless the buyer says 'with every right belonging to it', or says 'I bought it with its appurtenances', or says 'I bought it with every small or large thing in the residence or from the residence'; because the residence has resemblance to a courtyard house and resemblance to a house; for it is a noun for what comprises rooms, a roofed courtyard, a kitchen, in which a man lives with his family, with some deficiencies because it has no stable. Due to its resemblance to a courtyard house, the upper floor is included as a subordinate when mentioning subordinates like saying 'with every right' etc. And due to its resemblance to a house, the upper floor is not included in the residence without mentioning subordinates. And it is included in the purchase of a courtyard house even if he does not mention any of that; because 'courtyard house' is a noun for what is surrounded by boundaries of walls and includes houses, residences, and an unroofed courtyard, and the upper floor is from it, so it is included without mention, just as the latrine is included in the purchase of a courtyard house without being specifically mentioned. Likewise, the well, the trees in its courtyard, and the garden in it are included. In al-Kafi: this detail is based on the custom of the people of Kufa. In our custom, the upper floor is included in all, whether purchased by the name of house, residence, or courtyard house, because every dwelling, small or large, is a 'khana' (dwelling), so the custom of its people is considered in every region and every era. (Ramz al-Haqaiq: 62/2, 63) (Fath) 'with every right': i.e. if he bought a house above which is a house, the upper floor is not included even if he says 'I bought with every right' unless he explicitly states it. (Miskin) 'nor in the purchase': i.e. the upper floor is not included in the purchase etc. 'like the latrine': i.e. just as the latrine - which is the toilet - is included in the purchase of a courtyard house, not the sunshade, without being specifically mentioned. (Ayni) With a dhammah like a description, called in Persian 'sabat': the sunshade is not included in the sale of a courtyard house according to Abu Hanifa, whether its opening is in the courtyard house or not. According to the two companions, it is included without mentioning rights if its opening is in the courtyard house, because it is of the appurtenances of the courtyard house like the latrine and the upper floor. And according to the Imam, it is outside the boundaries, built on public airspace, so it takes its ruling, thus it is not included except with mention of rights and the like. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on rights (Bab al-Huquq) explains which appurtenances are automatically included when purchasing different types of real property in Hanafi law. The ruling is primarily a statement of the law with reasoning based on linguistic definitions and custom.

The key distinction is between three terms: bayt (a single room or house of one roofed unit), manzil (a residence consisting of multiple rooms, a roofed courtyard, and a kitchen, but lacking a stable), and dar (a courtyard house bounded by walls, containing rooms, residences, and an unroofed courtyard). 

- When buying a bayt, the upper floor (al-ulw) is NOT included even if the buyer says 'with every right' (bikulli haqq). It requires explicit mention. Reason: a bayt is a single roofed space; an upper floor is similar, so it cannot be subordinate to its like.
- When buying a manzil, the upper floor is NOT included unless the buyer uses a phrase like 'with every right belonging to it' (bikulli haqqin huwa lahu), 'with its appurtenances' (bimarafiqihi), or 'with every small and great thing in it or from it' (bikulli qalilin wa kathirin huwa fihi aw minhu). This is because a manzil has characteristics of both a bayt and a dar.
- When buying a dar, the upper floor IS included automatically, like the latrine (kanif), well, trees, and garden, without needing mention. The dar's boundaries encompass all that is within its walls.

An exception is the sunshade (zullah/sabat): According to Abu Hanifa, it is never included in the sale of a dar because it is built on public airspace, outside the boundaries. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is included if its opening faces into the dar.

The commentary from al-Kafi notes that this detailed distinction is based on the custom of Kufa. In other lands and eras, the custom of the people is followed: generally, the upper floor is included regardless of the term used.

The passage also explains that these rights are mentioned after the main issues of sales because they are ancillary, and there is a connection to riba: the excess in riba is haram, while the excess here (rights attached to the sold item) is halal.

Key Terms
العلو (al-ulw): The upper floor or upper room in a multi-story building.
بكل حق (bikulli haqq): Legal expression meaning 'with every right' – used to include all appurtenant rights in a sale.
مرافق (marafiq): Appurtenances or conveniences attached to the property, such as rights of way or water shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, under the custom of Kufa, if someone buys a 'bayt' (house) without any additional words, is the 'ulw' (upper floor) included?
A) Yes, always included because it is part of the house.
B) No, it is not included because a bayt cannot have a subordinate like itself.
C) It depends on whether the buyer says 'bikulli haqq'.
D) It depends on the size of the bayt.

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of the upper floor in a property sale hinges on the type of property and custom, not on a generic phrase like 'with every right'.

The Sunshade Dispute: Abu Hanifa vs. the Two Companions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inclusion of sunshade in dar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a sunshade (zullah) is included in the sale of a dar, and state the practical consequence and fatwa position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa excludes the sunshade; the Sahibayn include it if its opening is in the dar.

While the upper floor and latrine are automatically included in the sale of a dar, the sunshade (zullah) built over public airspace creates a disagreement. This lesson explores the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and how custom resolves the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed structure
The sunshade (zullah or sabat) is a roof-like structure built over a public road, attached to the dar, with an opening into the dar.
It is outside the walls of the dar.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
The sunshade is not included in the sale of the dar, regardless of whether its opening is in the dar, because it is built on public airspace (outside the boundaries).
It takes the ruling of the road.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The sunshade is included if its opening is in the dar, as it is a customary appurtenance like the latrine and upper floor.
Its attachment to the dar makes it part of the property's rights.
4
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa: built outside boundaries → takes ruling of public road. Sahibayn: serves the dar and is attached → included like other appurtenances.
The dispute hinges on whether the physical connection (opening) or the jurisdictional boundary (walls) determines inclusion.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Practically, under Abu Hanifa the buyer does not own the sunshade unless specified; under the Sahibayn it is automatically included. Later fatwa follows local custom; in many places, sunshades are considered part of the dar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحقوق أي في بيان أحكامها العلو لا يدخلُ بشراء بيت بكلّ حق، وبشراء منزل إلا بكل حق هو له أو بمرافقه، أو بكل قليل وكثير هو فيه أو منه، ودخل بشراء دار كالكنيف لا الظُّلّة . أي أو يقول فحينئذ يدخل العلو العلو وإن لم يذكر شيئاً من ذلك باب الحقوق وهي ما يتبع المبيع والثمن من الحقوق المعهودة (عيني)] لما فرغ من بيان ما هو أصل في البيع، وهو المبيع والثمن ذكر في هذا الباب ما يتبعهما من الحقوق المعهودة وله مناسبة خاصة بالربا؛ لأن بابه بیان فضل هو حرام، وهنا بيان فضل على المبيع هو حلال، وأخر الحقوق؛ لأنها توابع، فيليق ذكرها بعد مسائل البيوع. (عيني، فتح)
Chapter of Rights: i.e. explaining their rulings. The upper floor is not included in the purchase of a house (bayt) even with 'with every right', nor in the purchase of a residence (manzil) except with 'every right belonging to it' or 'with its appurtenances' or 'with every small and great thing in it or from it'. And it is included in the purchase of a courtyard house (dar) like the latrine, not the sunshade. That is, if he says... then the upper floor is included even if he does not mention any of that. Chapter of Rights: they are what follows from the sold item and the price of the customary rights. When he finished explaining what is the basis in sale, which is the sold item and the price, he mentioned in this chapter what follows them of the customary rights. And it has a special connection to riba because its chapter is explaining an excess that is haram, and here is an explanation of an excess on the sold item that is halal. And he delayed rights because they are ancillary, so it is appropriate to mention them after the issues of sales. (Fath) Its parts: The upper floor because 'house' is a noun for a single roofed structure suitable for dwelling, and the upper floor is similar to it, and a thing is not a subordinate to its like, so it is not included without explicit textual mention. If he says 'with every right belonging to it', likewise it is not included in the purchase of a residence, unless the buyer says 'with every right belonging to it', or says 'I bought it with its appurtenances', or says 'I bought it with every small or large thing in the residence or from the residence'; because the residence has resemblance to a courtyard house and resemblance to a house; for it is a noun for what comprises rooms, a roofed courtyard, a kitchen, in which a man lives with his family, with some deficiencies because it has no stable. Due to its resemblance to a courtyard house, the upper floor is included as a subordinate when mentioning subordinates like saying 'with every right' etc. And due to its resemblance to a house, the upper floor is not included in the residence without mentioning subordinates. And it is included in the purchase of a courtyard house even if he does not mention any of that; because 'courtyard house' is a noun for what is surrounded by boundaries of walls and includes houses, residences, and an unroofed courtyard, and the upper floor is from it, so it is included without mention, just as the latrine is included in the purchase of a courtyard house without being specifically mentioned. Likewise, the well, the trees in its courtyard, and the garden in it are included. In al-Kafi: this detail is based on the custom of the people of Kufa. In our custom, the upper floor is included in all, whether purchased by the name of house, residence, or courtyard house, because every dwelling, small or large, is a 'khana' (dwelling), so the custom of its people is considered in every region and every era. (Ramz al-Haqaiq: 62/2, 63) (Fath) 'with every right': i.e. if he bought a house above which is a house, the upper floor is not included even if he says 'I bought with every right' unless he explicitly states it. (Miskin) 'nor in the purchase': i.e. the upper floor is not included in the purchase etc. 'like the latrine': i.e. just as the latrine - which is the toilet - is included in the purchase of a courtyard house, not the sunshade, without being specifically mentioned. (Ayni) With a dhammah like a description, called in Persian 'sabat': the sunshade is not included in the sale of a courtyard house according to Abu Hanifa, whether its opening is in the courtyard house or not. According to the two companions, it is included without mentioning rights if its opening is in the courtyard house, because it is of the appurtenances of the courtyard house like the latrine and the upper floor. And according to the Imam, it is outside the boundaries, built on public airspace, so it takes its ruling, thus it is not included except with mention of rights and the like. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on rights (Bab al-Huquq) explains which appurtenances are automatically included when purchasing different types of real property in Hanafi law. The ruling is primarily a statement of the law with reasoning based on linguistic definitions and custom.

The key distinction is between three terms: bayt (a single room or house of one roofed unit), manzil (a residence consisting of multiple rooms, a roofed courtyard, and a kitchen, but lacking a stable), and dar (a courtyard house bounded by walls, containing rooms, residences, and an unroofed courtyard). 

- When buying a bayt, the upper floor (al-ulw) is NOT included even if the buyer says 'with every right' (bikulli haqq). It requires explicit mention. Reason: a bayt is a single roofed space; an upper floor is similar, so it cannot be subordinate to its like.
- When buying a manzil, the upper floor is NOT included unless the buyer uses a phrase like 'with every right belonging to it' (bikulli haqqin huwa lahu), 'with its appurtenances' (bimarafiqihi), or 'with every small and great thing in it or from it' (bikulli qalilin wa kathirin huwa fihi aw minhu). This is because a manzil has characteristics of both a bayt and a dar.
- When buying a dar, the upper floor IS included automatically, like the latrine (kanif), well, trees, and garden, without needing mention. The dar's boundaries encompass all that is within its walls.

An exception is the sunshade (zullah/sabat): According to Abu Hanifa, it is never included in the sale of a dar because it is built on public airspace, outside the boundaries. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is included if its opening faces into the dar.

The commentary from al-Kafi notes that this detailed distinction is based on the custom of Kufa. In other lands and eras, the custom of the people is followed: generally, the upper floor is included regardless of the term used.

The passage also explains that these rights are mentioned after the main issues of sales because they are ancillary, and there is a connection to riba: the excess in riba is haram, while the excess here (rights attached to the sold item) is halal.

Key Terms
الظلة (az-zullah): A sunshade or roof-like structure built over a public road, attached to a building.
الكنيف (al-kanif): The latrine or toilet, which is included automatically in the sale of a dar.
مفتح (maftah): The opening or entrance of a structure, e.g., the door of the sunshade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is the sunshade not included in the sale of a dar?
A) It is built on public airspace, outside the boundaries.
B) It is not a customary appurtenance.
C) It is too small to be considered.
D) The buyer did not ask for it.

Key Takeaway
The sunshade dispute illustrates how physical boundaries and custom can influence the transfer of appurtenances in Hanafi property law.

Inclusion of Rights of Way, Drainage, and Water Share in Sale and Lease
Ruling on automatic inclusion of these rights in sales vs leases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between when rights of way (tariq), drainage (masil), and water share (shurb) are automatically included in a contract of sale versus a lease, and apply the Hanafi rule that lease requires inclusion for benefit.

Core Idea
In lease, essential rights are automatic; in sale, they need explicit mention.

This lesson examines a critical distinction in Hanafi contract law: the automatic inclusion of essential rights in leases versus their exclusion in sales. The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains that while a sale of property does not automatically transfer rights of way, drainage, and water share, a lease does—because the very purpose of a lease is use and benefit. Understanding this principle is key to drafting valid contracts and avoiding disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rights and the default rule in sale
List the three rights: tariq (private right of way), masil (drainage channel), shurb (water share). Know that in a sale, these are not included unless the buyer uses an expression like 'with every right belonging to it' or 'with its appurtenances'.
The default rule is non-inclusion in sale because these rights are considered separate from the physical boundaries of the property.
2
Recognize the exception for lease and its rationale
Contrast with lease (ijarah): these rights are automatically included because lease is contracted for benefit (intifa'), and benefit cannot be realized without access, drainage, and water. The Hanafi position relies on istihsan to override the general rule.
The inclusion is necessary for the validity of the lease contract.
3
Understand the condition for drainage in lease
Note that the automatic inclusion of masil in lease is conditional: it applies only when the drainage channel is not within private property. If it is in private property, it requires explicit mention. Similarly, the snow disposal area (mistaq al-thalj) is not included without mention.
This exception preserves the rights of property owners.
4
Apply the rule to a dispute scenario
Consider a case: the buyer mentions 'rights' but the seller denies the existence of a right of way. The buyer does not automatically get it; he must provide proof. However, the buyer has the option to return the property due to defect ('ayb). Likewise, if there are beams from another property on the sold premises, the seller must remove them if they are his; if they belong to a third party, it is a defect.
This shows the practical consequence of non-inclusion.
5
Distinguish private vs public rights
Note that the 'tariq' discussed is a private easement. A road to a non-through alley (sikka ghayr nafidha) or a public road is automatically included in the sale, as it is not a limited private right.
Public rights are not subject to the same restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يدخل الطريق والمسيل والشرب إلا بنحو كل حق هو له، بخلاف الإجارة فيها. الخاص أي إلا أن يقول هو له ونحو ذلك بأن يقول : بمرافقه أو بكل قليل. (عيني ) ولا يدخل الطريق إلخ إلى قوله بنحو كل حق؛ لأن هذه الأشياء تابعة من وجه من حيث إنها تقصد للانتفاع بالمبيع دون عينها، وأصل من وجه باعتبار تصور وجودها بدون المبيع، فلا تدخل إلا بذكر الحقوق أو المرافق؛ لأن كلاً منها خارج عن الحدود، فكانت تابعة، فتدخل بذكر التوابع، والمراد الطريق الخاص في ملك إنسان، أما الطريق إلى سكة غير نافذة أو إلى طريق عام، فيدخل، فإن ذكر المشتري الحقوق وقال البائع: ليس للدار المبيعة طريق فالمشتري لا يستحق الطريق بغير حجة، لكن له أن يردها بالعيب، وكذلك لو كانت جذوع دار أخرى على الدار المبيعة، فإن كانت للبائع، يؤمر برفعها؛ لأن تخليص المبيع واجب على البائع، وإن كانت لغيره كان بمنزلة العيب، فله الرد. (عيني، فتح) والمسيل: هو موضع جري المطر وغيره. والشرب: بكسر المعجمة هو النصيب من الماء أي لا يدخل هذه الأشياء في بيع المسكن والأرض بدون ذكر الحقوق. (عيني) بخلاف الإجارة: أي يدخل الطريق والمسيل والشرب في الإجارة من غير ذكر كل حق ونحوه إذا استأجر داراً أو أرضاً؛ لأن الإجارة تعقد للانتفاع بخلاف البيع؛ لأنه قد يكون للتجارة، ولهذا لو استثنى هذه عن عقد الإجارة تبطل؛ إذ لا يمكن الانتفاع بالعين المستأجرة إلا بهذه الأشياء، فوجب دخولها فيها تصحيحاً للعقد، إلا أن دخول المسيل في الإجارة مقيد بما إذا لم يكن في ملك خاص، فإن كان لا يدخل المسيل حينئذ، ولا مسقط الثلج في الملك الخاص إلا إذا ذكر بكل حق ونحوه، ويدخل قدر الحمام والآلات المتصلة بالبيت كالباب وغيره. (عيني، فتح)
And the right of way, drainage channel, and water share are not included except with something like 'every right belonging to it', contrary to lease in them. 'Al-khas' meaning except if he says 'belonging to it' and the like, by saying 'with its appurtenances' or 'with every small [thing]'. (Ayni) 'And the right of way etc.' up to his saying 'with something like every right' because these things are subordinate in one sense (they are intended for the benefit of the sold item not their own essence) and original in another sense (they can be conceived existing without the sold item); so they are not included except with mention of rights or appurtenances; because each of them is outside the boundaries, so they are subordinate, so they are included by mentioning subordinates. What is meant is the specific right of way in the property of a person. As for the road to a non-through alley or to a public road, it is included. If the buyer mentions 'rights' and the seller says 'the sold courtyard house has no right of way', then the buyer does not deserve the right of way without proof, but he has the right to return it due to defect. Likewise if there are beams of another courtyard house on the sold courtyard house; if they belong to the seller, he is ordered to remove them because delivering the sold item free is obligatory on the seller. If they belong to another, it is like a defect, so he has the right to return. (Ayni, Fath) 'Al-masil' is the place where rainwater and other water flows. 'Al-shurb' with a kasra on the first letter is the share of water. i.e. these things are not included in the sale of a dwelling or land without mention of rights. (Ayni) 'Contrary to lease': i.e. the right of way, drainage channel, and water share are included in lease without mentioning 'every right' etc. when one leases a courtyard house or land; because lease is contracted for benefit unlike sale, because sale may be for trade. For this reason, if he excludes these from the lease contract, it becomes void, because it is not possible to benefit from the leased property except through these things, so their inclusion is necessary for the validity of the contract. Except that the inclusion of the drainage channel in lease is conditioned on it not being in private property; if it is, then the drainage channel is not included then. Nor is the snow disposal area in private property included except if he mentions 'with every right' etc. And the size of the bathroom and the appliances attached to the house like the door etc. are included. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on rights (Bab al-Huquq) and focuses on three specific rights: tariq (right of way/path), masil (drainage channel for rainwater etc.), and shurb (share of water/right to water). The ruling is that these are not automatically included in the sale of a dwelling or land unless the buyer uses an expression like 'with every right belonging to it' (bikulli haqqin huwa lahu) or 'with its appurtenances' (bimarafiqihi). The reason given is that these rights are subordinate from one angle (they are intended for the benefit of the sold property, not for their own sake) but also independent from another angle (they can exist separately from the sold property). Since they are outside the physical boundaries of the property (kharja 'an al-hudud), explicit mention is required for their inclusion.

An important exception: in contracts of lease (ijarah), these rights are automatically included even without mention. The rationale (talil) is that lease is fundamentally a contract for benefit (al-ijarah tu'aqidu lil-intifa'), whereas sale can be for other purposes like trade. Since the leased property cannot be beneficially used without access, drainage, and water, excluding them would render the contract invalid. Therefore, their inclusion is necessary for the validity of the contract (tashihan lil-aqd).

The passage also provides further details:
- The 'right of way' discussed is a specific private right of way (al-tariq al-khass). Public roads or alleys are included automatically.
- If the buyer mentions rights and the seller denies the existence of a right of way, the buyer does not automatically get it; he must provide proof, but he has the option to return the property for defect.
- Similarly, if there are beams (judh'u) of another house projecting onto the sold property, if they belong to the seller, he must remove them; if they belong to a third party, it is a defect giving the right of return.
- For drainage, in lease contracts, it is included only if it is not in private property. If it is in private property, it is not included without mention. The same applies to snow disposal area (mistaq al-thalj) on private property.
- Additionally, items like the size of a bathroom (qadr al-hammam) and attached fixtures like doors are included in the sale/lease.

The commentary by al-Ayni and al-Fath explains these rulings with reasoning based on the nature of the rights and the purpose of the contracts.

Key Terms
الطريق (al-tariq): The specific right of way that provides access to the property; a private easement.
المسيل (al-masil): The drainage channel or course for rainwater and other water flow.
الشرب (al-shurb): The share of water or right to draw water.
الإجارة (al-ijarah): A lease contract for benefit, distinguished from sale which may be for trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are tariq, masil, and shurb automatically included in a lease but not in a sale?
A) Because lease is a weaker contract and needs more protections.
B) Because lease is contracted for benefit, and benefit cannot be attained without these rights, whereas sale may be for trade.
C) Because these rights are more important in lease than in sale.
D) Because the buyer can always negotiate separately.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, rights of way, drainage, and water share are automatically included in a lease but not in a sale, unless explicitly mentioned.

Ancillary Rights in Sale vs Ijarah: The Hanafi Distinction
Rules on inclusion of ancillary rights in sale vs Ijarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on whether ancillary rights (path, watercourse, water share) are automatically included in a sale and in an Ijarah contract, and apply the rule to practical scenarios.

Core Idea
In sale, ancillary rights must be explicitly mentioned; in Ijarah, they are automatic because benefit requires them.

The Hanafi school treats certain rights—like a private path, a watercourse, and a water share—as subsidiary to the main property. However, their inclusion in a contract depends on the nature of the contract. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq lays out the default rules and the underlying reasoning (ta'lil). We will see why a buyer must explicitly claim these rights, while a lessee gets them automatically, and what exceptions exist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and the right
Determine whether the transaction is a sale or an Ijarah, and which ancillary right is being considered: private path (tariq), watercourse (masil), or water share (shurb).
These are the three specific rights discussed.
2
State the rule for sale
In a sale, these rights are not automatically included. The buyer must explicitly mention them by saying 'every right' or 'with its appurtenances'. If not mentioned, the buyer cannot claim them but may return the property if their absence is a defect.
Exception: a path leading to a cul-de-sac or public road is automatically included.
3
State the rule for Ijarah
In an Ijarah (lease), these rights are automatically included because the contract is for benefit (intifa'). Without them, the leased property cannot be used effectively. Excluding them would void the contract.
However, if the watercourse is in a private property not part of the lease, it is not included.
4
Apply to similar issues
Other items like beams from the seller's house on the sold property, snow removal, and attached fixtures (bathroom, door) follow similar logic. The seller is obliged to remove his own beams; if they belong to a third party, it is a defect. Attached fixtures are always included.
5
Trace the talil (reasoning)
The difference arises because these rights are conceptually separate from the property but necessary for its use. In sale, the buyer can choose to accept the property without them; in Ijarah, the lessee's sole purpose is benefit, making their inclusion essential for the validity of the contract.

باب الحقوق
إلا بكل حق هو له، ولا يدخل الطريق والمسيل والشرب إلا بنحو كل حق هو له، أي إلا أن يقول هو له ونحو ذلك بأن يقول: بمرافقه أو بكل قليل. ولا يدخل الطريق إلخ إلى قوله بنحو كل حق؛ لأن هذه الأشياء تابعة من وجه من حيث إنها تقصد للانتفاع بالمبيع دون عينها، وأصل من وجه باعتبار تصور وجودها بدون المبيع، فلا تدخل إلا بذكر الحقوق أو المرافق؛ لأن كلاً منها خارج عن الحدود، فكانت تابعة، فتدخل بذكر التوابع، والمراد الطريق الخاص في ملك إنسان، أما الطريق إلى سكة غير نافذة أو إلى طريق عام، فيدخل، فإن ذكر المشتري الحقوق وقال البائع: ليس للدار المبيعة طريق فالمشتري لا يستحق الطريق بغير حجة، لكن له أن يردها بالعيب، وكذلك لو كانت جذوع دار أخرى على الدار المبيعة، فإن كانت للبائع، يؤمر برفعها؛ لأن تخليص المبيع واجب على البائع، وإن كانت لغيره كان بمنزلة العيب، فله الرد. والمسيل: هو موضع جري المطر وغيره. والشرب: بكسر المعجمة هو النصيب من الماء أي لا يدخل هذه الأشياء في بيع المسكن والأرض بدون ذكر الحقوق. بخلاف الإجارة: أي يدخل الطريق والمسيل والشرب في الإجارة من غير ذكر كل حق ونحوه إذا استأجر داراً أو أرضاً؛ لأن الإجارة تعقد للانتفاع بخلاف البيع؛ لأنه قد يكون للتجارة، ولهذا لو استثنى هذه عن عقد الإجارة تبطل؛ إذ لا يمكن الانتفاع بالعين المستأجرة إلا بهذه الأشياء، فوجب دخولها فيها تصحيحاً للعقد، إلا أن دخول المسيل في الإجارة مقيد بما إذا لم يكن في ملك خاص، فإن كان لا يدخل المسيل حينئذ، ولا مسقط الثلج في الملك الخاص إلا إذا ذكر بكل حق ونحوه، ويدخل قدر الحمام والآلات المتصلة بالبيت كالباب وغيره. الأشياء
Chapter of Rights: Except with every right that belongs to him, and the path, watercourse, and right of water are not included except by something like 'every right that belongs to him', i.e., unless he says 'it is his' or similar by saying 'with its appurtenances' or 'with every little thing'. And the path etc. up to his saying 'by something like every right'; because these things are subsidiary in one sense in that they are intended for benefiting from the sold item without their own essence, and they are an origin in another sense considering the possibility of their existence without the sold item, so they are not included except by mentioning the rights or appurtenances; because each of them is outside the boundaries, so they are subsidiary, so they are included by mentioning the subsidiaries. What is meant is the specific path within a person's ownership. As for the path to a dead-end alley or to a public road, it is included. If the buyer mentions the rights and the seller says 'the sold house has no path', the buyer does not deserve the path without proof, but he has the right to return it due to defect. Similarly, if there are beams of another house on the sold house, if they belong to the seller, he is ordered to remove them because freeing the sold item is obligatory upon the seller; if they belong to someone else, it is like a defect, so he may return it. Al-Masīl: it is the place where rainwater and other water flows. Al-Shurb: with kasra on the shīn is the share of water. So these things are not included in the sale of a dwelling and land without mentioning the rights. Contrary to Ijarah: i.e., the path, watercourse, and right of water are included in Ijarah without mentioning every right and similar, when one rents a house or land, because Ijarah is contracted for benefit, unlike sale because it may be for trade. Hence if he excludes these from the Ijarah contract, it becomes void, since it is not possible to benefit from the leased property except through these things, so their inclusion is necessary to validate the contract. However, the inclusion of the watercourse in Ijarah is conditioned upon it not being in a specific private property; if it is, then the watercourse is not included, nor does snow removal in private property unless mentioned by 'every right' and similar. And the area of the bathroom and the fixtures attached to the house like the door and others are included.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses the inclusion of incidental rights in sale and Ijarah. The author states a general rule: in a sale, ancillary rights such as a private path (tariq), watercourse (masil), and water share (shurb) are not automatically included unless the buyer explicitly mentions 'every right' or similar phrases like 'with its appurtenances'. This is because these rights are conceptually separate from the sold property, though they are necessary for its use. The commentary clarifies that a 'private path' refers to one within the seller's property, while paths leading to a cul-de-sac or public road are included automatically. If the seller denies the existence of a path, the buyer cannot claim it without proof but may return the property due to defect. Similarly, if another's beams rest on the sold house, the seller must remove them if they are his, otherwise it is a defect. The rule changes for Ijarah: in a lease, these rights are automatically included without explicit mention because the purpose of Ijarah is benefit (intifa'), and without these rights the benefit is impossible. Excluding them would void the contract. However, the inclusion of the watercourse is conditional: if it lies in private property not part of the lease, it is not included. Snow removal from private property also requires explicit mention. Finally, items like the bathroom and fixtures attached to the house (e.g., doors) are included in both sale and Ijarah.

Key Terms
طريق (tariq): The private path within the property of the seller leading to the sold property.
مسيل (masil): A watercourse for rainwater or other water flow.
شرب (shurb): The share or right of water.
حقوق (huquq): In this context, ancillary rights such as path, watercourse, water share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a person rents a house without mentioning the right to use a private path?
A) The path is not included because it was not mentioned.
B) The path is automatically included because Ijarah is for benefit.
C) The contract is void because the path is necessary.
D) The tenant must obtain the path separately.

Key Takeaway
Ancillary rights like path, watercourse, and water share are not automatically included in a sale unless explicitly mentioned, but they are automatically included in an Ijarah to ensure the lessee's benefit.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Evidence, Confession, and Contradiction in Claims
States the legal principles of evidence, confession, and contradiction in claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three principles of evidence, confession, and contradiction, and apply them to claims of ownership, freedom, divorce, and lineage.

Core Idea
Evidence binds all; confession binds only self; contradiction bars ownership claims but not hidden matters like freedom.

In this passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, the author lays down three foundational rules for judicial proceedings: the transitive effect of court evidence, the personal limitation of confession, and the nuanced treatment of contradictory claims. These principles govern how proof operates in sales and claims of right, with important exceptions for freedom, divorce, and lineage where knowledge may be hidden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three principles
Read the opening lines: 'البينة حجة متعدية، لا الإقرار والتناقض يمنع دعوى الملك لا الحرية والطلاق والنسب'. Identify that evidence is transitive, confession is not, and contradiction prevents ownership claims but not freedom, divorce, or lineage.
These are the core dicta; the rest is expansion.
2
Analyze evidence transitivity
Focus on the explanation: evidence becomes a proof only by the judge's ruling, and the judge has general authority, so the ruling binds everyone. Note the examples: a buyer claiming a slave is free or belongs to another, and the ruling extends to all people.
Contrast with confession which binds only the confessor.
3
Examine confession limitation
Understand why confession is not transitive: it is a proof by itself, not dependent on judgment, and the confessor has authority only over himself. The example: if a buyer confesses the slave belongs to another, he cannot recover the price from the seller.
When both evidence and confession exist, confession prevails unless there is a need (like recovering price).
4
Understand contradiction and its exceptions
Contradiction prevents the judge from accepting a claim of ownership because both statements cannot be true. But for freedom, divorce, and lineage, contradiction is excused because these matters may be hidden (i.e., known only to the master/husband/parent). Study the examples for each exception.
The principle is: public acts (like buying) create a presumption; hidden matters override contradiction.
5
Apply the rules to new scenarios
Consider a variation: what if a buyer first claims the slave is his own property, then later claims he belongs to the seller? This is contradictory in ownership, so the later claim is not accepted unless supported by the seller's own confession.
The key is whether the contradiction is about ownership or one of the exempt categories.

كتاب البيوع باب الاستحقاق أي في بيان أحكامه. البينة حجة متعدية، لا الإقرار والتناقض .
البينة حجة متعدية إلى الغير حتى تظهر في حق كافة الناس لأنها لا تصير حجة إلا بالقضاء وللقاضي ولاية عامة فينفذ قضاؤه في حق الكافة كما إذا اشترى أمة، فادعى أنها حرة الأصل، أو أنها ملك فلان وقد أعتقها أو دبرها أو استولدها قبل شرائها، والأمة تدعي، وأقام المشتري البينة على دعواه تقبل بينته؛ لأن التناقض في الحرية وفروعها لا يمنع صحة الدعوى، ويرجع المشتري بالثمن على البائع، وإذا ثبت حريتها في حق البائع ثبتت في حق كافة الناس، حتى لا تسمع دعوى أحد، والحاصل أن القضاء بالبينة حجة متعدية إلى الكافة في العتق والنكاح والنسب والولاء، وفي الملك المؤرخ من وقت التاريخ لا قبله.
لا الإقرار: أي ليس الإقرار بحجة متعدية إلى الغير، حتى يقتصر على المدعي، يعني ليس الإقرار بحجة متعدية؛ لأن الإقرار حجة بنفسه لا يتوقف على القضاء، وللمقر ولاية على نفسه دون غيره، فيقتصر عليه، وهذا أصل لفروع كثيرة منها ما أشار إليها المصنف بقوله مبيعة ولدت إلخ منها: أنه إذا اشترى عبداً ثم ظهر له مستحق بالبينة، فإنه يرجع على البائع بالثمن؛ لأنه يثبت الملك بالبينة من الابتداء، فيتعدى إلى الكل أي البائع والمشتري، ولو أقر المشتري بالعبد لرجل أنه مستحق له لا يرجع بالثمن على البائع؛ لأن إقراره قاصر عليه، فيثبت به الملك في المقر به ضرورة صحة الإخبار وقد اندفعت الضرورة بإثبات الملك للمقر له، فلا يظهر الاستحقاق في حق المشتري في الرجوع بالثمن على البائع، وعند اجتماع الإقرار والبينة يقضى بالإقرار على الأظهر إلا عند الحاجة فبالبينة والحاجة رجوعه بالثمن.
والتناقض يقال: تناقض الكلامان تدافعا كأن كل واحد نقض الآخر، وفي كلامه تناقض إذا كان بعضه يقتضي إبطال بعض، واختلفوا في اشتراط كون الكلام عند القاضي فمنهم من شرطه ومنهم من شرط كون الثاني عند القاضي فقط، كما لو اشترى أمة ثم ادعى أنها ملك فلان وفلان يدعيها، وأقام المشتري بينة على دعواه ليرجع بالثمن لا تقبل؛ لأن إقدامه على الشراء دليل على أنها ملك البائع، فإذا ادعى لغيره كان متناقضا، ولو برهن على إقرار البائع أنه ملك فلان يقبل؛ لعدم التناقض، وإنما يمنع التناقض في الكلام دعوى الملك؛ لأن القاضي لا يمكنه أن يحكم بالكلام المتناقض؛ إذ أحدهما ليس بأولى من الآخر فسقطا، وهذا أيضاً أصل لفروع كثيرة وموضعها في كتاب الدعوى من "المبسوط".
لا الحرية: أي التناقض لا يمنع دعوى الحرية، كما لو اشترى أمة وقبضها ثم ادعى أنها معتقة فلان، وأقام البينة، تقبل، ويرجع بالثمن على البائع وكما لو ادعى المكاتب بدل الكتابة، ثم برهن على أن مولاه أعتقه قبل الكتابة، فإنه تقبل بينته، ورجع بما أدى، فالتناقض لا يمنع صحة الدعوى في دعوى العتق؛ لأنه أمر يجري فيه الخفاء؛ إذ هو أمر يتفرد به المولى، فربما لا يعلم العبد إعتاقه، ثم يعلم بعد ذلك، فيعفى التناقض دفعاً للحرج عن العبد.
والطلاق أي ولا يمنع التناقض دعوى الطلاق، كالمرأة إذا اختلعت نفسها من زوجها، ثم أقامت بينة على أن زوجها طلقها ثلاثاً قبل الخلع، فإنه تقبل بينتها، ولها أن تسترد بدل الخلع؛ لأنها وإن كانت متناقضة؛ إذ إقدامها على الخلع يدل على عدم الطلاق فتكون بدعوى الطلاق متناقضة، ومع هذا يعفى التناقض لاستقلال الزوج بإيقاع الثلاث عليها من غير أن يكون لها علم بذلك، وقيدنا المسألة بالثلاث؛ لأن في ما دون الثلاث يمكن أن يقيم الزوج البينة أنه قد تزوجها بعد الطلاق الذي أثبتته المرأة ببينتها قبل يوم أو يومين، وأما في الثلاث فلا يمكن.
والنسب: أي ولا يمنع التناقض أيضاً دعوى النسب، كما إذا باع عبداً ولد عنده، وقبضه المشتري وباعه المشتري من آخر، ثم ادعاه البائع الأول أنه ابنه تسمع دعواه، ويبطل البيع الأول والثاني؛ لأنه يدعي البنوة، فعلم حرا، وبيع الحر باطل وعدم منع التناقض في دعوى النسب أن النسب يبتني على العلوق، وهو مما يخفى أنه باع فيعفى فيه التناقض.
Book of Sales. Chapter of Claim of Right, i.e. explaining its rulings. The evidence is a transitive proof, not the confession and the contradiction. The evidence is a transitive proof to others until it appears regarding all people, because it does not become a proof except by judgment, and the judge has general authority, so his judgment is effective regarding everyone, as if he bought a slave-girl and claimed she was originally free, or that she belonged to so-and-so who had freed her, or made her mudabbara, or made her umm walad before his purchase, and the slave-girl also claims, and the buyer presents evidence for his claim: his evidence is accepted because contradiction in freedom and its branches does not prevent the validity of the claim, and the buyer recovers the price from the seller. And when her freedom is established regarding the seller, it is established regarding all people, so that no one's claim is heard. The conclusion is that judgment based on evidence is transitive to everyone regarding emancipation, marriage, lineage, and patronage, and regarding ownership dated from the time of the date, not before. Not the confession: i.e. the confession is not a transitive proof to others, so it is confined to the claimant. That means the confession is not a transitive proof because the confession is a proof by itself, not dependent on judgment, and the confessor has authority over himself but not over others, so it is confined to him. This is a principle for many branches, among which the author pointed out saying 'a sold slave-girl gave birth' etc. Including: if he bought a slave then a right-claimant appeared with evidence, he recovers the price from the seller because ownership is established by evidence from the beginning, so it is transitive to all, i.e. the seller and the buyer. If the buyer confessed the slave to a man that he is entitled to it, he does not recover the price from the seller because his confession is confined to him, so ownership is established in the confessed thing necessarily for the validity of the report, and the necessity has been removed by establishing ownership for the confessee, so the claim of right does not appear regarding the buyer's recourse for the price from the seller. When confession and evidence meet, judgment is given by confession according to the most apparent view, except when there is need, then by evidence, and the need is his recourse for the price. Contradiction: it is said: two statements contradict each other, each rebutting the other, and in his statement there is contradiction if some of it necessitates invalidation of some. They differed about the condition of the statement being before the judge: some conditioned it, some conditioned only the second statement before the judge. For example, if he bought a slave-girl then claimed she belonged to so-and-so, and so-and-so claimed her, and the buyer presented evidence for his claim to recover the price: it is not accepted because his proceeding to buy indicates she was the seller's property, so if he claimed for another he is contradictory. If he presented evidence of the seller's confession that she belongs to so-and-so, it is accepted due to lack of contradiction. Contradiction only prevents a claim of ownership in speech because the judge cannot judge on contradictory speech since one is not more worthy than the other, so they both fall. This is also a principle for many branches, their place is in the Book of Claims from al-Mabsut. Not freedom: i.e. contradiction does not prevent a claim of freedom, as if he bought a slave-girl and took possession, then claimed she was freed by so-and-so, and presented evidence: it is accepted, and he recovers the price from the seller. Similarly, if a mukatib claimed the substitute for kitaba, then proved that his master freed him before the kitaba: his evidence is accepted and he recovers what he paid. So contradiction does not prevent the validity of the claim in a claim of emancipation because it is a matter in which hiddenness occurs, as it is a matter exclusively done by the master, so perhaps the slave does not know his emancipation, then knows afterwards, so contradiction is excused to remove hardship from the slave. And divorce: i.e. contradiction does not prevent a claim of divorce, as a woman who divorced herself from her husband by khul', then presented evidence that her husband divorced her three times before the khul': her evidence is accepted, and she may recover the khul' compensation because although she is contradictory since her proceeding to khul' indicates absence of divorce, so she would be contradictory by claiming divorce, yet contradiction is excused because the husband alone effects the triple divorce upon her without her knowledge. We restricted the issue to triple divorce because in less than three, the husband can present evidence that he married her after the divorce she proved with her evidence a day or two before, but in triple divorce it is not possible. And lineage: i.e. contradiction also does not prevent a claim of lineage, as if he sold a slave born in his possession, and the buyer took possession and sold him to another, then the first seller claimed he was his son: his claim is heard, and the first and second sales are void because he claims sonship, so he is known to be free, and the sale of a free person is void. The non-prevention of contradiction in a claim of lineage is because lineage is based on conception, which is a hidden matter, so contradiction is excused.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on claims of right (al-istiḥqāq) in the book of sales. The author begins by stating three fundamental principles: first, that evidence (al-bayyinah) presented in court and upheld by the judge's ruling is transitive—it affects all people, not just the parties to the suit. This is because the judge has general authority, and his judgment is binding on everyone. Second, a confession (al-iqrār) is not transitive; it only binds the person who confesses, because it is an admission against oneself and does not require a court ruling. Third, contradiction (al-tanāquḍ) in a party's statements before the court prevents the acceptance of a claim regarding ownership, but does not prevent claims regarding freedom, divorce, or lineage. The reason is that ownership is a matter that the claimant should be aware of; if he contradicts himself, his claim is unreliable. However, freedom, divorce, and lineage are matters that may be hidden from the person, so contradiction is excused to avoid hardship. The passage then provides detailed examples for each principle. For evidence transitivity: a man buys a slave-girl and later claims she is free or belongs to someone else; if he proves it with evidence, the ruling applies to all, and he can recover the price from the seller. For confession limitation: if the buyer confesses the slave belongs to another, he cannot recover the price because his confession does not bind the seller. For contradiction: if a buyer initially acts as if the slave-girl belongs to the seller (by buying her) but then claims she belongs to another, his claim is contradictory and not accepted unless he proves the seller's own confession. The exceptions for freedom, divorce, and lineage are illustrated with examples: a woman claiming divorce after khul', a slave claiming freedom after kitaba, and a seller claiming his former slave is his son. In each case, the contradiction is overlooked because the truth was hidden. This passage is foundational for understanding the law of evidence and claims in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence or proof presented in court that becomes effective only after the judge's ruling; it is transitive to all people.
الإقرار (al-iqrār): A confession or admission by a person against himself; it is a proof by itself without needing a judge's ruling, and it only binds the confessor.
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ): Contradiction in a person's statements or actions that indicates inconsistency; it prevents claims of ownership but not claims of freedom, divorce, or lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the passage say that contradiction does not prevent a claim of divorce, as in the case of a woman who initiated khul' and later proves her husband divorced her three times before?
A) Because the woman is not a party to the marriage contract.
B) Because the husband alone can issue triple divorce, and the wife may not know about it.
C) Because khul' is a different type of divorce.
D) Because the judge always favors the wife's evidence.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, evidence after judgment binds all, confession binds only the confessor, and contradiction bars ownership claims but not claims of freedom, divorce, or lineage due to hidden facts.

Ruling on the Child of a Sold Slave-Girl When Claimed by Evidence or Confession
Applies the principles of evidence and confession to the child of a sold slave-girl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the child of a sold slave-girl when a third party claims her by evidence or by confession, and explain why the difference arises based on the nature of the proof.

Core Idea
Evidence is transitive, so the child follows; confession is limited, so the child does not follow unless claimed.

In this passage, we examine a specific application of the principles of evidence (bayyinah) and confession (iqrar). A slave-girl is sold, later gives birth, and then a claimant appears. How does the nature of the proof affect the child? The answer turns on whether the proof is 'mutlaq' (absolute) or 'qasirah' (limited), a key distinction in Hanafi procedure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct situations: (1) the slave-girl is claimed with evidence (bayyinah) from the claimant, and (2) the buyer confesses (iqrar) that the slave-girl belongs to someone else. Note the different legal consequences for the child.
2
Analyze the ruling on the child in each scenario
In the evidence scenario, the child follows the mother to the claimant. In the confession scenario, the child does not follow – it remains with the buyer unless the confessee also claims the child.
The key difference is the transitive nature of evidence versus the limited nature of confession.
3
Understand the underlying rationale
Evidence (bayyinah) is an absolute proof – it establishes the claimant's ownership from the origin, so the child, being connected to the mother at that time, becomes his. Confession (iqrar) is a limited proof – it only binds the confessor and establishes ownership only as needed to validate the report; since the child was born after the sale, it is not automatically included.
The buyer recovers the price in the evidence scenario because the seller sold what was not his; in the confession scenario, the buyer does not recover because the confession does not prove the seller's liability.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the evidence scenario
There is a difference of opinion on whether a separate judgment (qada) on the child is required. Some say the judgment on the mother suffices because the child is a dependent. Others, including Imam Muhammad, require a separate judgment because at the time of the ruling the child is an independent entity. The text states the latter is the more correct view (al-asahh).
Imam Muhammad's statement about appendages (zawa'id) supports the need for a separate judgment.
5
Apply the ruling to the buyer's right to recover the price
In the evidence scenario, the buyer recovers the full price from the seller because the claim is proven and evidence is transitive. In the confession scenario, the buyer does not recover the price because the confession is limited and does not implicate the seller.
This shows the practical consequences of the two types of proof.

مبيعة : أي فلو كانت جارية مبيعة مثلا. فاستحقت أي فظهر لها مستحق ببينة. يتبعها ولدها [ أي يتبع المبيعة ولدها يعني يأخذها المستحق مع ولدها. (عيني)] لأن البينة حجة مطلقة فإنها كاسمها مبينة، فيظهر بها ملكه من الأصل والولد كان متصلاً بها، فيكون له، ثم يدخل الولد تبعا بالقضاء في الأم؛ لأنه تبع لها، فيكتفي بقضاء الأم، وقيل: يشترط القضاء بالولد علي حدة، وهو الأصح؛ لأن الولد يوم القضاء أصل بنفسه منفصل عن الأم فلابد من الحكم مقصودا، ويدل عليه ما قال محمد: إن القاضي إذا لم يعلم بالزوائد لا تدخل الزوائد بالحكم، وكذا الولد إذا كان في يد غيره لا يدخل تحت الحكم بالأم تبعا. (عيني، كفاية)
وإن أقر بها إلخ : أي إذا ولدت المبيعة عند المشتري وأقر المشتري بها لرجل فيأخذ المقر له الجارية فقط، ولا يأخذ ولدها؛ لأن الإقرار حجة قاصرة فيثبت بها الملك المخبر به ضرورة صحة الإخبار، وقد اندفعت بإثباته بعد انفصال الولد عن الأم، فلا يكون الولد له فيرجع بالثمن في الصورة الأولى التي ثبت فيها الاستحقاق بالبينة وهي حجة متعدية بخلاف هذه الصورة؛ فإنه لا يرجع لثبوت الاستحقاق بالإقرار وهي حجة قاصرة، وفي "النهاية": لا يتبعها الولد في الإقرار إذا لم يدع عليه المقر له، أما إذا ادعاه كان الولد له أيضاً؛ لأن الظاهر أنه له. (عيني، فتح)
A sold slave-girl: i.e. if there was, for example, a sold slave-girl. Then she is claimed, i.e. a claimant appears with evidence. Her child follows her, i.e. the child of the sold slave-girl follows her, meaning the claimant takes her along with her child. Because evidence is an absolute proof, for it is as its name indicates 'clarifying', so through it his ownership from the origin becomes apparent, and the child was connected to her, so it belongs to him. Then the child enters in the mother by way of dependency through the judgment because it is a dependent of hers, so the judgment on the mother suffices. And it is said: separate judgment on the child is required, and this is the most correct, because on the day of judgment the child is a principal in itself, separate from the mother, so judgment on it as an intended object is necessary. This is supported by what Muhammad said: if the judge does not know about the appendages, the appendages do not enter by the judgment. Similarly, if the child is in the hands of another, it does not enter under the judgment on the mother by dependency.
And if he confessed it, etc.: i.e. if the sold slave-girl gave birth in the possession of the buyer, and the buyer confessed her to a man, then the confessee takes only the slave-girl and does not take her child, because confession is a limited proof, so ownership of the thing reported is established by it necessarily for the validity of the report, and the necessity has been removed by establishing it after the child separated from the mother, so the child does not belong to him. So he recovers the price in the first scenario where the claim of right was established by evidence, which is transitive, unlike this scenario, for he does not recover because the claim of right is established by confession, which is a limited proof. In al-Nihaya: the child does not follow her in confession if the confessee does not claim it, but if he claims it, the child is also his, because the apparent indication is that it is his.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage applies the earlier principles to a specific case: a slave-girl is sold, and after the sale she gives birth. Later, a claimant appears asserting ownership. If the claimant proves ownership with evidence (bayyinah), the child accompanies the mother and is taken by the claimant. The reasoning is that evidence is a 'mutlaq' (absolute) proof, establishing ownership from the origin, and the child was part of the mother at that time. However, there is a disagreement within the Hanafi school: some say the judge's ruling on the mother automatically includes the child as a dependent, so no separate judgment is needed. Others, and this is the preferred view (al-asahh), require a separate judgment for the child because at the time of the ruling, the child is an independent entity separate from the mother. Imam Muhammad's statement supports this: if the judge is unaware of appendages, they are not included. If the child is in another's hands, it requires its own judgment. If, instead of evidence, the buyer confesses that the slave-girl belongs to another person (iqrar), then only the slave-girl is given to the confessee, not her child. This is because confession is a 'qasirah' (limited) proof—it only affects the confessor and establishes ownership only to the extent necessary to validate the admission. Since the child was born after the confession (or after the purchase), the necessity is removed, and the child remains with the buyer. The buyer can recover the price from the seller in the evidence scenario (because the claim of right is proven, and evidence is transitive, so it also establishes that the seller sold what was not his), but he cannot recover the price in the confession scenario because the confession is limited and does not establish the seller's liability. However, al-Nihaya notes that if the confessee also claims the child, then the child goes with the mother because the apparent custody (zahir) indicates so.

Key Terms
مبيعة (mubīʿah): A sold female slave (or any sold female animal) that is the subject of the transaction.
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Claim of right or ownership by a third party against a buyer or possessor, based on evidence or confession.
ولد (walad): Child born to the slave-girl after the sale; its status follows the mother's according to the nature of the proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a sold slave-girl gives birth and then is claimed by a third party with evidence (bayyinah), what happens to the child?
A) The child remains with the buyer because it was born after the sale.
B) The child goes to the claimant because evidence establishes ownership from the origin.
C) The child is divided equally between buyer and claimant.
D) The child is set free because its status is unclear.

Key Takeaway
When a claimed slave-girl is proven by evidence, her child follows; when by confession, the child does not follow unless also claimed.
