Child of Sold Slave-Girl Follows Mother Only with Clear Evidence
Ruling on the child of a sold slave-girl when the mother is reclaimed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between bayyinah and iqrār in determining whether a child follows a reclaimed slave-girl, and state the practical consequence for the buyer's right to recover the price.

Core Idea
Bayyinah is transitive proof binding on all; iqrār is limited proof binding only on the one who acknowledges.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from the chapter of sales: when a sold slave-girl gives birth and then a third party proves ownership of the mother, does the child automatically belong to the claimant? The answer depends on the type of proof—clear evidence (bayyinah) versus mere acknowledgment (iqrār). Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly applying Hanafi commercial law and for grasping the deeper principle that proofs differ in their scope and effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two situations: (1) the claimant proves ownership with bayyinah (clear evidence); (2) the buyer acknowledges (iqrār) the child to a third party. State the ruling for each: with bayyinah the child follows the mother; with iqrār it does not.
The second scenario also includes an exception: if the acknowledgee claims the child, then it follows.
2
Understand the legal nature of each proof
Bayyinah is described as 'absolute proof' (ḥujjah muṭlaqah) that binds all parties—it is transitive (muta'addiyah). Iqrār is 'limited proof' (ḥujjah qāṣirah) that only binds the one making the acknowledgment.
This distinction is a core Hanafi principle: the effect of a proof depends on its scope.
3
Apply the proof to the child's status
With bayyinah, the claimant's ownership is established from the origin, so the child (born before separation) belongs to the claimant. With iqrār, the acknowledgment occurs after the child's birth, so the child is not included unless separately claimed.
The text also notes an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a separate judgment on the child is required when bayyinah is used.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some scholars say judgment on the mother suffices because the child is a dependent (taba'). But the preferred view (al-aṣaḥḥ) is that a separate judgment is needed because the child is an independent entity at the time of judgment.
The practical outcome: if the judge does not rule separately, the child remains with the buyer unless separately claimed.
5
Determine the price recovery consequence
In the bayyinah case, the buyer recovers the price from the seller because the proof is transitive and the sale is rescinded. In the iqrār case, the buyer does not recover the price because iqrār is limited.
This difference underscores the hierarchical weight of proofs in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبيعة ولدت فاستحقت ببينة يتبعها ولدها وإن أقر بها لا.

أي فلو كانت جارية مبيعة مثلا. فاستحقت أي فظهر لها مستحق ببينة. يتبعها ولدها [ أي يتبع المبيعة ولدها يعني يأخذها المستحق مع ولدها. (عيني)] لأن البينة حجة مطلقة فإنها كاسمها مبينة، فيظهر بها ملكه من الأصل والولد كان متصلاً بها، فيكون له، ثم يدخل الولد تبعا بالقضاء في الأم؛ لأنه تبع لها، فيكتفي بقضاء الأم، وقيل: يشترط القضاء بالولد على حدة، وهو الأصح؛ لأن الولد يوم القضاء أصل بنفسه منفصل عن الأم فلابد من الحكم مقصودا، ويدل عليه ما قال محمد: إن القاضي إذا لم يعلم بالزوائد لا تدخل الزوائد بالحكم، وكذا الولد إذا كان في يد غيره لا يدخل تحت الحكم بالأم تبعا. (عيني، كفاية)

وإن أقر بها إلخ : أي إذا ولدت المبيعة عند المشتري وأقر المشتري بها لرجل فيأخذ المقر له الجارية فقط، ولا يأخذ ولدها؛ لأن الإقرار حجة قاصرة فيثبت بها الملك المخبر به ضرورة صحة الإخبار، وقد اندفعت بإثباته بعد انفصال الولد عن الأم، فلا يكون الولد له فيرجع بالثمن في الصورة الأولى التي ثبت فيها الاستحقاق بالبينة وهي حجة متعدية بخلاف هذه الصورة؛ فإنه لا يرجع لثبوت الاستحقاق بالإقرار وهي حجة قاصرة، وفي "النهاية": لا يتبعها الولد في الإقرار إذا لم يدع عليه المقر له، أما إذا ادعاه كان الولد له أيضاً؛ لأن الظاهر أنه له. (عيني، فتح)
A sold [slave-girl] gives birth, then she is reclaimed by clear evidence: her child follows her. And if he acknowledges [the child] to someone, the child does not follow. [Commentary:] Meaning if a sold slave-girl gave birth, then a claimant appeared with clear evidence (bayyinah). Her child follows her, because bayyinah is an absolute proof—as its name indicates, it makes things clear—so his ownership from the origin becomes apparent, and the child was connected to her, so it is his. Then the child enters as a dependent in the judgment on the mother; since it is dependent on her, a judgment on the mother suffices. It is said that a separate judgment on the child is required, and this is the more correct view, because on the day of judgment the child is an independent entity separate from the mother, so a specific ruling is needed. This is supported by Muhammad's statement: if the judge does not know of the appendages, they are not included in the judgment. Similarly, if the child is in the hand of another, it does not fall under the judgment on the mother as a dependent. And if he acknowledges [the child] to someone: i.e., if the sold slave-girl gave birth in the buyer's possession and the buyer acknowledged the child to a man, then the acknowledged recipient takes only the slave-girl, not her child, because acknowledgment (iqrār) is a limited proof; it establishes ownership only as reported out of necessity for the validity of the report. Since ownership is established after the child's separation from the mother, the child does not belong to him. In the first case (with bayyinah) the buyer recovers the price because the proof is transitive; unlike this case where he does not recover because the proof is limited. In al-Nihāyah: the child does not follow in acknowledgment unless the acknowledged recipient claims it, then the child is also his, because the apparent condition is that it is his.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This lesson addresses the status of a child born to a sold slave-girl when a third party proves (istiḥqāq) ownership of the mother. Two scenarios are distinguished: (1) The claimant proves ownership via bayyinah (clear evidence): the child follows the mother and is taken together with her. The reason is that bayyinah is a general proof (ḥujjah muṭlaqah) that establishes the claimant's ownership from the origin, and the child was born while the mother was in the seller's ownership, so the child belongs to the claimant. There is a difference of opinion on whether the judge must separately rule on the child. The preferred view (al-aṣaḥḥ) is that a separate judgment is needed because the child is an independent entity at the time of judgment. (2) The buyer acknowledges (iqrār) the child to a third party: only the mother is taken, not the child, because iqrār is a limited proof (ḥujjah qāṣirah) that only establishes ownership as reported. Since the acknowledgment occurred after the child's separation, the child is not included. Furthermore, the buyer does not recover the price from the acknowledging party. The text also notes an exception: if the acknowledged recipient also claims the child, then the child is included because the apparent is that the child belongs to him. This ruling highlights the Hanafi distinction between bayyinah (transitive proof) and iqrār (limited proof).

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Clear evidence (usually testimony of two male witnesses) that establishes ownership absolutely.
الإقرار (al-iqrār): A party's own admission or acknowledgment of a right, which is binding only on that party.
تبع (taba'): Dependence; the child's legal status as following the mother's ownership.
حجة متعدية (ḥujjah muta'addiyah): Transitive proof that affects third parties and establishes rights against all.
حجة قاصرة (ḥujjah qāṣirah): Limited proof that only binds the one who produces it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the buyer does not own the child.

Key Takeaway
Bayyinah is transitive and includes the child; iqrār is limited and does not, unless separately claimed.

Liability of a Former Slave Who Instructed His Own Purchase
Ruling on liability when a free person instructs his own purchase pretending to be a slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditions under which a former slave is liable for the purchase price when found free, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A slave who claims to be a slave and instructs his purchase guarantees his own freedom when the seller is absent.

This passage addresses a challenging scenario: a person buys someone who claims to be a slave but turns out free. The key question is who bears the loss when the seller cannot be found. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement between the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf. Understanding this dispute illuminates how Hanafi jurists balance reliance, guarantee, and the nature of contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Conditions
Read the first part of the passage to see what act triggers liability: the slave must say both 'Buy me' AND 'I am a slave'. If only one is said, no liability. Also note the seller's status: if present or in a known absence, the buyer recovers from the seller, not the slave.
The condition of the seller's absence is 'ghaybah munqati'ah' (disconnected absence) — not just unknown location.
2
State the General Ruling
When the seller is absent in a disconnected absence, the buyer can recover the price from the former slave (who is now free). The former slave then recovers from the seller.
This is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
3
Examine the Dalil for the Majority View
The majority argue that the former slave, by claiming he was a slave, effectively guaranteed (ḍamān) his own freedom. The buyer relied on his statement. Since recourse to the seller is impossible, the slave is liable to prevent harm (gharar). The slave is not a volunteer because he was compelled to free himself.
Key term: ḍamān al-salāmah (guarantee of safety).
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's Dissent
Abu Yusuf holds that there is no basis for liability because the transaction is a sale (mu'āwaḍah) and the buyer's recourse should be against the seller alone. The slave merely gave information, not a guarantee. The only bases for liability are exchange or guarantee (kafālah), neither present.
Abu Yusuf equates this case with the scenario where the slave only said 'Buy me' without claiming to be a slave — in that case, no liability by consensus.
5
Distinguish from the Pledge (Rahn) Case
The passage contrasts sale with pledge: if a slave says 'Pledge me, I am a slave' and the pledgee does so, then finds him free, the pledgee cannot recover from the slave even if the pledgor is absent. This is because a pledge is a security contract, not a commutative contract. Liability for defect of title arises only in exchange contracts.
This distinction reinforces the majority's reasoning: in sale, the slave's statement induces reliance, but in pledge, the reliance is on the pledgor, not the slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال عبد لمشتر: اشترني فأنا عبد فاشترى، فإذا هو حر، فإن كان البائع حاضرا أو غانا غيبة معروفة، فلا شيء على العبد، وإلا رجع المشتري على العبد بالثمن إذا ظفر به، والعبد على البائع بخلاف الرّهن.

لرجل لا. وإن قال عبد لمشتر: اشترني فأنا عبد فاشترى، فإذا هو حر، فإن كان البائع حاضرا أو غانا غيبة معروفة، فلا شيء على العبد، وإلا رجع المشتري على العبد بالثمن إذا ظفر به، والعبد على البائع بخلاف الرّهن. لرجل : لا أي لا يتبعها ولدها فيرجع الثمن في الصورة الأولى دون الثانية. (ط) اشترني فأنا عبد: قيد بالقيدين؛ لأنه لو قال: أنا عبد ولم يأمره بشراء أو قال: اشترني ولم يقل أنا عبد لا رجوع عليه بشيء؛ لأن الحر يشترى تخليصاً كالأسير، وقد لا يجوز شراء العبد كالمكاتب، فلم يوجد منه ما يدل على الضمان، وهذا في قولهم جميعاً، وقوله: "رجع المشتري على العبد بالثمن عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد؛ لأن المقر بالعبودية ضمن سلامة نفسه، فالمشتري اعتمد على أمره، وأقر أمره أنه عبده؛ إذ القول قوله في الحرية، فيكون تغريراً للمشتري، وهو الضمان، فيجعل ضامناً بالثمن عند تعذر رجوعه على البائع دفعاً للضرر والغرر. وقوله: "والعبد على البائع" مع أنه لم يأمره بالضمان؛ لأن العبد قضى ديناً عنه، وهو مضطر فيه، فلا يكون متبرعاً، كمعير الرهن إذا أعار عبد الرهن للمستعير بالدين، ثم قضى المعير دينه لتخليص العبد، فإنه مضطر فيه، فلا يكون متبرعاً. وعند أبي يوسف: لا يرجع المشتري على العبد بشيء؛ لأن ضمان الثمن بالمعاوضة أو بالكفالة، ولم يوجد سبب واحد منهما، فصار كما إذا قال: اشترني فقط، أو قال: أنا عبد، و لم يزد على ذلك. (عيني، فتح، كافي) فاشترى: أي الرجل العبد بناء على كلامه. (عيني) معروفة : يعني يدرى مكانه ويرجى حضوره أين هو. وإلا إلخ أي وإن لم يدر البائع أين هو، وغاب غيبة منقطعة. (ط، عيني) بخلاف الرهن [إذا وجد حرا، حيث لا يرجع المرتمن على العبد ولو غاب الراهن غيبة منقطعة. (ط)] أي بخلاف ما لو قال العبد: ارتهنني فأنا عبد، فارتهنه، فإذا هو حر، حيث لا يرجع المرتمن على العبد بحال، سواء كان الراهن حاضراً أو غائباً عنه؛ لأن الرهن ليس بعقد معاوضة، بل هو وثيقة لاستيفاء حقه، ألا ترى أنه يجوز في موضع لا يجوز فيه المبادلة كثمن الصرف ورأس مال السلم والمسلم فيه، وإذا لم يكن عقد معاوضة لا يجعل الأمر به ضمانا للسلامة؛ إذ هو في ضمن عقد المعاوضة. (عيني، فتح)
And if a slave said to a buyer: 'Buy me, for I am a slave,' and he bought [him], and then he turned out to be free, then if the seller was present or absent in a known absence, there is nothing on the slave. Otherwise, the buyer can recover the price from the slave if he finds [him], and the slave can recover from the seller. Unlike a pledge. [Commentary:] ... (detailed explanation of the conditions and the opinion of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad vs Abu Yusuf).
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a case where a person instructs another to buy him, claiming to be a slave, but is actually free. The ruling depends on two conditions: (1) the buyer must have said both 'Buy me' and 'I am a slave'—if he said only one, there is no recourse; (2) if the seller is present or reachable, the buyer recovers from the seller. If the seller is absent in a disconnected absence (ghaybah munqati'ah), then according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, the buyer can recover the price from the former slave (who is now free), because he represented himself as a slave, effectively guaranteeing his own freedom. The former slave then recovers from the seller, as the seller received the price without right. This is not considered a voluntary payment because the former slave was compelled (muḍṭarr) to free himself from bondage. Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that there is no basis for liability (ḍamān) because the transaction is a sale, and the buyer's recourse should be against the seller alone. The case is contrasted with a pledge (rahn): if a person says 'Pledge me, for I am a slave,' and the pledgee does so, then finds him free, the pledgee cannot recover from the former slave under any circumstances, because a pledge is a security contract, not a commutative contract (mu'āwaḍah). This distinction underscores the Hanafi principle that liability for defect of title arises primarily in exchange contracts.

Key Terms
الغرر (al-gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a transaction; here, the buyer relied on the slave's statement, leading to a risky purchase.
المعاوضة (al-mu'āwaḍah): Commutative contract, i.e., an exchange of countervalues, such as sale.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Guarantee or liability; here, the former slave becomes a guarantor of the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the reason the former slave is liable for the price when the seller is absent?
A) Because the slave committed fraud.
B) Because the slave guaranteed his own freedom by his statement.
C) Because the buyer had no other option.
D) Because the slave was the actual seller.

Key Takeaway
A slave who claims to be a slave and instructs his purchase becomes a guarantor of his freedom; if the seller is untraceable, the buyer can recover the price from him.

Partial Reclamation After Settlement for an Unknown Right
Ruling on return of settlement money upon partial reclamation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on returning settlement money when a portion of a disputed property is reclaimed, distinguishing between claims for an unknown vs. known share.

Core Idea
Settlement for an unknown right is valid; if part is reclaimed, return proportionally; if all, return fully.

This lesson examines a common scenario in property disputes: a person claims an unspecified share in a house, settles for a fixed sum, and later a third party proves ownership of part of the house. The Hanafi school provides clear rules for whether the settlement money must be returned in whole or in part, based on the nature of the original claim.

Guided Path
1
Classify the initial claim
Determine whether the claimant asserted an unknown right (majhul) or a known quantity (e.g., one-quarter). If the claim was for an unknown right, the settlement is treated as a release (ibrā'). If for a known quantity, it is treated as a sale of that specific share.
The text explicitly says: if he claimed a known amount like a quarter, he does not recoup as long as that amount remains in his hand.
2
Determine the extent of reclamation
Identify how much of the property was reclaimed by the third party: part or all. This distinction changes the return obligation.
The ruling is conditional on partial reclamation; full reclamation triggers a full return of the settlement amount.
3
Apply the return rule
For an unknown claim: if part is reclaimed, return a proportional amount; if all is reclaimed, return the full amount. For a known claim: no return as long as the originally claimed share remains; if less remains, return proportionally.
The reasoning is certainty (yaqīn): when all is reclaimed, the claimant took compensation for something he did not own.
4
Understand the underlying rationale
The validity of settlement over an unknown right is based on the principle that releasing a right (ibrā') is permissible even if the amount is unknown, because the ignorance concerns what is given up, which does not lead to dispute. The return rule protects the settling owner from unjust enrichment.
This rationale is the talīl (reasoning) provided in the text.

[Commentary:] He claimed a right, i.e., an unknown amount, because if he claimed a known amount like a quarter, he does not recoup as long as that amount remains in his hand; if less remains, he recoups proportionally. 'Some of it is reclaimed' is conditional on partial reclamation; if all is reclaimed, he recoups everything he paid, because we are certain he took compensation for what he did not own. Commentary
This lesson deals with a situation where a person claims an unspecified share in a house and reaches a settlement with the owner for a fixed sum of 100 dirhams. Later, part of the house is reclaimed by a third party (istiḥqāq). The ruling: if the claim was for an unknown share (e.g., 'a right'), then the claimant must return a proportional amount of the settlement if part is reclaimed. If the entire house is reclaimed, he returns the full sum because he clearly received compensation for something he did not own. The text contrasts this with a claim for a known quantity (e.g., one-quarter): in that case, the claimant is considered to have purchased that specific share, so he does not return any settlement amount as long as he still possesses that share; only if the reclaimed part reduces his share below that does he return proportionally. This ruling confirms the Hanafi position that settlement (ṣulḥ) for an unknown right (majhūl) in exchange for a known countervalue is valid, because releasing a debt or right that is unknown is permissible—the ignorance concerns what is relinquished, which does not lead to dispute since it is a waiver.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-ṣulḥ): Settlement or compromise to end a dispute, often involving payment in exchange for dropping a claim.
المجهول (al-majhūl): An unknown or unspecified right or thing; here, the claimant's share in the house is not specified at the time of settlement.

Liability of a Free Person Who Directs His Purchase as a Slave
Ruling with proof on a free person's liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a free person who directs his own purchase as a slave becomes liable for the price, and explain the Hanafi reasoning based on istihsan.

Core Idea
A free person who misrepresents himself as a slave and directs purchase is liable for the price to prevent harm, but only when the seller is unreachable.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a subtle case: a free person tells a buyer 'Buy me, I am a slave.' The buyer does so, only to discover the seller is actually free. Does the free person owe the price? The Hanafi school splits: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad impose liability when the seller is absent without trace, while Abu Yusuf denies liability altogether. The reasoning turns on the nature of misrepresentation (taghrir) and the principle of preventing harm (istihsan).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the conditional ruling
The act: a free person directs a buyer to purchase him as a slave. The ruling: liability for the price depends on the seller's presence or absence.
The ruling has two distinct scenarios.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
Scenario 1: seller is present or his whereabouts are known → no liability on the free person. Scenario 2: seller is absent in a disconnected absence (ghayba munqati'a) → the free person is liable, and he then recovers from the seller.
The key condition is the seller's reachability.
3
Understand the proof (istihsan)
The free person's admission and order constitute a misrepresentation (taghrir) that misleads the buyer. Since the buyer relied on this, the free person effectively guarantees the slave's freedom. Liability is imposed to prevent harm and uncertainty for the buyer.
This is a hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) to cases where a person undertakes safety.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Yusuf holds that liability only arises from exchange or guarantee, which are absent. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) view is relied upon in fatwa.
The practical difference: in the absentee seller scenario, Abu Yusuf would deny recourse against the free person.
5
Contrast with the mortgage case
In a mortgage, even if the mortgagor is absent, the mortgagee cannot recover from a free person who claimed to be a slave, because mortgage is not a contract of exchange.
This distinction reinforces that liability here is based on the exchange nature of the sale.

Chapter of Claim. And if a slave says to a buyer: 'Buy me, for I am a slave,' and he buys, and behold he is free: if the seller is present or absent in a known absence, then nothing is owed by the slave; otherwise the buyer recovers the price from the slave, and the slave (recovers) from the seller, unlike a mortgage. Commentary: The expression 'Buy me, for I am a slave' is restricted to both conditions; because if he only said 'I am a slave' without ordering purchase, or said 'Buy me' without saying 'I am a slave,' he does not recover anything. This is agreed upon. The buyer recovers the price from the slave according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because the one who admits to slavery warrants his own safety; the buyer relied on his order and his admission that he is his slave, since his word is accepted regarding freedom, so it is a misrepresentation causing liability, making him liable for the price when recourse to the seller is impossible, to prevent harm and uncertainty. And the slave (recovers) from the seller because the slave paid a debt on his behalf out of necessity, not as a volunteer. Abu Yusuf says: the buyer does not recover anything from the slave, because liability arises from exchange or guarantee, and neither exists.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, with commentary from 'Ayni, Fath al-Qadir, and al-Kafi, addresses the case where a free person directs a buyer to purchase him claiming to be a slave. The ruling is conditional: if the seller is present or absent but his whereabouts are known, the slave (the free person) is not liable because the buyer can recover from the seller. If the seller is absent in a disconnected absence (ghayba munqati'a), the buyer recovers the price from the free person, who in turn recovers from the seller. The commentary explains that the free person's statement is considered a misrepresentation (taghrir) that causes loss to the buyer, so he is liable by istihsan to prevent detriment. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that liability arises only from exchange or guarantee, which are absent. The passage also contrasts with a mortgage case where the mortgagee cannot recover from a free person who ordered the mortgage, because mortgage is not a contract of exchange.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): The emergence of a right or claim, here referring to the free person's right to be free and the buyer's right to recover the price.
تغرير (taghrīr): Misrepresentation leading to loss; the free person's claim of being a slave misleads the buyer.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or guarantee; here the free person becomes liable for the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, regardless of the seller's presence
B) Only if the seller is absent in a disconnected absence (ghayba munqati'a)
C) Only if the seller is present and known
D) Never, according to all Hanafi imams

Key Takeaway
A free person who directs his purchase as a slave is liable for the price only when the seller is absent with unknown whereabouts, based on istihsan to prevent harm from misrepresentation.

Claim of Right in a House and Settlement for Unknown Amount
Ruling on settlement for unknown claim and partial recovery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on settlement (ṣulḥ) for an unknown right and calculate the proportional refund when part of the property is reclaimed.

Core Idea
Settlement for an unknown claim is valid; refund upon reclamation depends on what was originally claimed.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common scenario: a person claims an unspecified right in a house and reaches a settlement for a fixed sum. The ruling clarifies two critical points: first, that such a settlement is valid in Hanafi law, and second, how to handle the situation when it later emerges that the claimant actually owned part of the house. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the distinction between claiming a specific right versus claiming the entire property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct cases: (1) Claimant claims an unknown right and settles, then part of the property is reclaimed. (2) Claimant claims the whole house and settles, then part is reclaimed.
The ruling for each case differs markedly.
2
Understand the validity condition
Settlement (ṣulḥ) for an unknown right is valid because ignorance in what is being dropped does not lead to dispute, according to Hanafi school. The validity of the underlying claim is a condition for the settlement against denial.
This is a distinctive Hanafi position.
3
Apply the refund rule
In the first scenario (unknown right), no refund is given to the defendant. In the second scenario (whole house claimed), the defendant recovers a proportionate share of the settlement based on the portion reclaimed.
The rationale: in the first case, the claim may still exist for the remaining part; in the second, the settlement was for the entire property.
4
Compute the proportionate share
If half the house is reclaimed, the defendant recovers half the settlement amount. Use the formula: refund = settlement amount × (reclaimed portion / total property).
This is a simple proportional calculation.

(Commentary: The claim is unknown, and the defendant denied it. 'Part is reclaimed' is qualified: if the whole is reclaimed, he recovers what he paid because we are certain he took compensation for what he did not own. Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with Fath al-Qadir commentary deals with a claim of an unknown right in a house followed by a settlement. The key points: (1) Settlement for an unknown claim (e.g., an unspecified right) in exchange for a known sum is valid in the Hanafi school because releasing an unknown right is permissible and does not lead to dispute. (2) If the claimant later proves partial ownership (istiḥqāq) of the property, he does not recover any of the settlement money unless he had claimed the entire house. If he claimed the whole house and then part is proven to be his, the defendant recovers a proportional amount of the settlement. The reasoning is that the settlement was for the entire house; if part is reclaimed, it becomes clear that the claimant did not own that portion, so the consideration must be returned proportionally.

Key Terms
صلح (ṣulḥ): Settlement or compromise, a contract resolving a dispute by mutual agreement.
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Emergence of a right, here meaning that part of the property is proven to belong to the claimant.
قسط (qisṭ): Proportionate share, used to calculate the refund.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, settlement for an unknown right is valid; partial reclamation of property after settlement triggers a proportional refund only if the original claim was for the entire property.

Section on Sale by an Unauthorized Person (Bay' al-Fuduli)
Dispute on validity of sale by fuduli

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a sale by an unauthorized person is suspended on ratification and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school validates a sale by a non-owner if the owner later ratifies; Shafi'i considers it absolutely void.

This section introduces the classical dispute over bay' al-fuduli. The Hanafi school, relying on hadith and istihsan, treats such a sale as suspended until ratification. The Shafi'i school, emphasizing the need for immediate authority, deems it void. Understanding this difference clarifies the Hanafi approach to contracts lacking initial authority.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and key terms
Identify the three core terms: fuduli (unauthorized person), mawquf (suspended), ijaza (ratification). The Hanafi ruling: the sale is concluded as valid but suspended on the owner's ratification.
The term 'fuduli' is derived from 'fadl' meaning excess, indicating one who meddles.
2
Identify the Shafi'i opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that such a contract is void ab initio because the agent lacks legal authority at the time of contract. Ratification cannot validate a void act.
The Shafi'i position is based on the principle that authority is a condition for the contract's validity.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof (dalil)
Two hadiths are cited: 'Urwa ibn Abi Ja'd purchased two sheep with one dinar, sold one, and the Prophet approved. Hakim ibn Hizam bought an udhiya with a dinar, profited, and the Prophet accepted. These show transactions beyond the mandate were not void.
The hadith of 'Urwa is narrated by Bukhari, Ahmad, and Abu Dawud.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school uses istihsan: ratification retroactively supplies the missing authority, similar to a guardian's subsequent consent. The contract is not void because it can be corrected.
The condition for suspension is that a potential ratifier exists at the time of contract; otherwise the contract is void.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi, if the owner ratifies, the sale is valid and the buyer owns the property. Under Shafi'i, even if the owner later agrees, the contract remains void and a new contract must be made.
This difference affects real estate transactions where immediate authority is disputed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ط، معدن)] أي بقسط المستحق؛ لأن الصلح على مائة وقع عن كل الدار، فإذا استحق منها شيء تبين أن المدعي لا يملك ذلك القدر، فيرد بحسابه من العوض، وتوضيحه أنه إذا ادعى كل دار معين فوقع الصلح على مائة، واستحق نصف الدار، رجع المدعى عليه بخمسين درهماً على المدعي. فصل: أي في بيع الفضولي، والفضول بضم الفاء جمع فضل غلب هذا الجمع في الاشتغال بما لا يعنيه ولا ولاية له فيه، وفي "المغرب": هو في اصطلاح الفقهاء من ليس بوكيل، وفتح الفاء خطاء، والأولى أن يقال: هو من ف يتصر في حق الغير بلا إذن شرعي. (فتح) ملك غيره: بلا أمره، توقف إن كان الغير بالغا عاقلا، وإلا لم ينعقد أصلا. (ط) فللمالك أن يفسخه: مطلقاً صريحا بأن قال: فسخت، أو دلالة بأن باع المعقود عليه من غيره، والسكوت بعد العلم لا يكون إجازة، وكذا قوله: "أو يجيزه" أي مطلقاً صريحا بأن قال: أجزت أو دلالة بأن قبض الثمن من مشتريه أو طلبه أو وهبه من المشتري أو تصدق به عليه، وقوله: "إن بقي العاقدان إلخ" شرط للإجازة؛ لأن الإجازة تصرف في العقد فلابد من قيامه وذلك لقيام الأشياء، والأصل فيه: أن كل تصرف صدر من الفضولي وله مجيز حال وقوعه انعقد موقوفاً على الإجازة عندنا، ولا يقع باطلاً، أما إذا لم يكن له مجيز لا يتوقف ويقع باطلا.
وقال الشافعي: تصرفات الفضولي كلها باطلة ولا تتوقف على الإجازة؛ لأنه صادر عن غير ولاية شرعية، ولنا: ما روي من حديث عروة بن أبي جعد البارقي "أن النبي أعطاه دينارين ليشتري له بهما شاة، فأشترى له بهما شاتين، فباع إحداهما وجاء بدينار وشاة، فدعا له بالبركة في بيعه، فكان لو اشترى التراب لربح فيه"، رواه البخاري وأحمد وأبو داود، وحديث حبيب بن أبي ثابت عن حكيم بن حزام أنه علي بعثه ليشتري له أضحية بدينار فاشترى له أضحية فربح فيها دينارا فاشترى مكانها فجاء بالأضحية والدينار لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ولو لم ينعقد البيع =
Section: Whoever sells the property of another, the owner may annul it (i.e., the sale, if he wishes). Commentary: This section concerns the sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli). The term 'fuduli' with damma on the fa' is a plural of 'fadl', denoting one who meddles in what does not concern him. In al-Mughrib: in the terminology of jurists, he is one who is not an agent; opening the fa' is incorrect. Better: one who acts in the right of another without legal permission. 'Property of another' means without his order; it is suspended if the other is a sane adult, otherwise it does not take effect at all. The owner may annul it absolutely, explicitly by saying 'I annul' or implicitly by selling the subject matter to another; silence after knowledge is not ratification. Or he may ratify it absolutely, explicitly by saying 'I ratify' or implicitly by taking the price, demanding it, giving it as a gift to the buyer, or giving it in charity. The condition 'if the two contractors remain' is necessary for ratification because ratification is an act concerning the contract, so the contract must still exist. The principle: every transaction by an unauthorized person that has a ratifier at the time of occurrence is concluded as suspended upon ratification according to us, and does not occur as void. But if there is no ratifier, it is not suspended and occurs void. Al-Shafi'i said: all transactions of an unauthorized person are void and not suspended upon ratification, because they issue from one without legal authority. Our proof: the hadith of 'Urwa ibn Abi Ja'd al-Bariqi that the Prophet gave him two dinars to buy a sheep; he bought two sheep, sold one, and came with a dinar and a sheep; the Prophet prayed for blessing in his sale, saying 'if he bought dust he would profit'. And the hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam...
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This section introduces the Hanafi doctrine of bay' al-fuduli (sale by an unauthorized person). The Hanafi position is that such a sale is suspended (mawquf) on the owner's ratification. If the owner ratifies, the sale becomes effective retroactively; if he annuls, it is void. This is based on istihsan and the principle that the contract can be validated by subsequent consent. The Shafi'i school holds such contracts absolutely void because the agent lacks authority at the time of contract. The Hanafis adduce two hadiths where companions acted beyond their mandate and the Prophet approved, implying that such transactions are not void ab initio. The passage also defines 'fuduli' and outlines the method of ratification or annulment.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): An unauthorized person who transacts in another's property without permission.
موقوف (mawqūf): Suspended; the contract's final effect depends on ratification.
إجازة (ijāza): Ratification; subsequent approval that validates a suspended contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli) is:
A) Absolutely void
B) Valid but suspended on the owner's ratification
C) Valid only if the owner was present
D) The same as a sale by an agent

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a sale by an unauthorized person as suspended on ratification, distinguishing it from the Shafi'i view of absolute voidness, based on hadith and the principle of istihsan.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Sale of Another's Property: Suspended Validity and the Fuduli Dispute
Ruling on sale by unauthorized person, conditions for ratification, and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds a fuduli sale as suspended (mawquf) rather than void, state the conditions for ratification, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A sale of another's property is suspended, not void — the owner's later ratification makes it fully effective.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents the Hanafi position on a critical area of contract law: when someone sells property they do not own. Unlike the Shafi'i school, which deems such transactions void, the Hanafi school treats them as suspended — valid in essence but awaiting the owner's consent. This ruling is built on strong hadith evidence and a careful analysis of harm and benefit. We will explore the core arguments, the conditions for ratification, and an intra-Hanafi dispute on a related sub-case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
Read the passage carefully to see that the fuduli sale is 'mawquf' (suspended) and not 'batil' (void). Understand the owner's options: cancel or ratify. Note the conditions for ratification (aql, bulugh, remaining of contractors and subject matter).
The term mawquf is key: it means the contract is concluded but its effect depends on later approval.
2
Follow the Shafi'i objection
Identify Imam al-Shafi'i's position: all fuduli dispositions are void because they lack legal authority. Locate his proof: the hadith prohibiting sale of what one does not own and the principle of avoiding gharar (deception).
This is a methodological difference: Shafi'i focus on the absence of permission at the time of contract.
3
Analyse the Hanafi evidence
Examine the two hadiths cited: the story of Urwa ibn Abi al-Ja'd and that of Hakim ibn Hizam. The Prophet did not reject these sales; in fact, he prayed for blessing. Understand how Hanafi usul uses these as proof of validity.
The Hanafi response also refutes the gharar claim: the sale is conditional, not deceptive.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi dispute
Examine the sub-case: a buyer frees the slave he bought from a fuduli, then the owner ratifies. The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say the freeing is valid by istihsan; Muhammad and Zufar say it is not, by qiyas. Understand the reasoning: ownership is established retroactively vs. needing complete ownership at the time of freeing.
Note that the mufta bihi position is the view of the two shaykhs.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Compare the practical outcomes: Hanafi: owner can still benefit from a fuduli’s work, and the contract can become binding. Shafi'i: the contract is dead; owner must start anew. For the freeing sub-case: the slave becomes free in the Hanafi view, but not in Muhammad's view.
The ikhtilaf has real-world impact on property transfers and manumission.

وقال الشافعي: تصرفات الفضولي كلها باطلة ولا تتوقف على الإجازة؛ لأنه صادر عن غير ولاية شرعية، ولنا: ما روي من حديث عروة بن أبي جعد البارقي "أن النبي أعطاه دينارين ليشتري له بهما شاة، فأشترى له بهما شاتين، فباع إحداهما وجاء بدينار وشاة، فدعا له بالبركة في بيعه، فكان لو اشترى التراب لربح فيه"، رواه البخاري وأحمد وأبو داود، وحديث حبيب بن أبي ثابت عن حكيم بن حزام أنه علي بعثه ليشتري له أضحية بدينار فاشترى له أضحية فربح فيها دينارا فاشترى مكانها فجاء بالأضحية والدينار لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ولو لم ينعقد البيع =
لم يجوزه النبي ولرده، ولأن ركن التصرف صدر من أهله مضافاً إلى محله ولا ضرر في انعقاده موقوفا فينعقد؛ لأن الأهلية بالعقل والمحلية بكون المال متقوما، وليس فيه ضرر على المالك؛ لأنه مخير في الفسخ والنفاذ، بل له فيه منفعة حيث سقط عنه مؤنة طلب المشتري ونفع للمتعاقدين بصون كلامهما عن الإلغاء، فإن قلت: ما لا يملكه كما نهى عن بيع المبيع قبل القبض وعن بيع الأبق؟ قلت: الكلام في انعقاد العقد أي الجواز وعدمه، وبيع المبيع قبل القبض ينعقد عندنا وإن كان فاسدا، وكذا الآبق في رواية، حتى لو سلّمه فلا يلزمنا، والمراد من الحديث أن صح يبيع شيئاً لا يملكه ثم يشتريه ويسلمه. فإن قلت: هذا غرر ونهى عليه عن بيع الغرر فيما رواه مسلم، قلت: لا نسلم أنه غرر؛ لأنه يتوقف على إجازة المالك، ولا يشترط بقاء الثمن لصحة الإجازة، إلا إذا كان عرضا فحينئذ يشترط بقاؤها أيضاً، وإذا أجازه المالك كان الثمن مملوكا له أمانة في يد الفضولي بمنزلة الوكيل حتى لا يضمن بالهلاك في يده، سواء هلك بعد الإجازة أو قبلها؛ لأن الإجازة اللاحقة كالوكالة السابقة وللفضولي أن يفسخ العقد قبل الإجازة دفعا للحقوق عن نفسه، وكذا للمشتري فسخ البيع قبل الإجازة تحرزا عن لزوم العقد. (عيني، فتح) وله: أي بقي المعقود له وهو المالك. (محشي) وبه أي والمعقود به وهو الثمن عرضا: أي متاعاً يتعين بالتعيين فصار كالمبيع. (محشي) وصح عتق مشتر إلخ أي إن غصب عبداً، فأعتقه المشتري ثم أجاز المالك بيع الغاصب، صح العتق عن المشتري استحساناً عند الشيخين، وعند محمد وزفر: لا يصح، وهو القياس؛ لأنه لا عتق بدون الملك؛ لقوله علي: "لا عتق فيما لا يملك ابن أدم"، والموقوف وإن أفاد الملك لكن مستنداً، وهو ثابت من وجه دون وجه، فلا يصلح شرطاً للإعتاق؛ لأن المصحح له الملك الكامل للحديث. ولهما: أن الملك يثبت موقوفاً بتصرف مطلق موضوع لإفادة الملك، فيتوقف الإعتاق مرتباً عليه، فينفذ بنفاذه، وصار كإعتاق المشتري من الراهن، فإنه يتوقف وينفذ بإجازة المرتمن البيع؛ لأن الأصل اتصال الحكم بالسبب، والتأخير لدفع الضرر عن المالك، والضرر في نفاذ الملك لا في توقفه ولا نسلم أن الإعتاق يحتاج إلى الملك وقت ثبوته، بل وقت نفاذه والمراد بالحديث العتق النافذ. (عيني، فتح بإجازة: أي بإجازة المالك لا بيعه: أي لا يصح بيع المشتري من الغاصب وإن أجاز المولى بيعه؛ لأنه بالإجازة ثبت للمشتري الأول، وهو البائع الثاني ملك بات فإذا طرء على ملك موقوف لغيره أبطله؛ لاستحالة اجتماع الملك البات والملك الموقوف في محل واحد. رمز الحقائق: ٦٨/٢] (فتح) فأجيز : أي البيع ثم أجاز المالك البيع.
The Book of Sales. Ruling on one who sold the property of another: he does not recoup anything; and if he claimed all of it, he recoups its portion. The defendant against the plaintiff, the house. Section. And whoever sells the property of another, the owner may cancel it. That is, the sale if he wishes. With the condition for the validity of the settlement; because claiming an unknown right is not valid due to the ignorance of the claim, such that even if he presents evidence it is not accepted, unless he claims the defendant's admission of the right, then the claim and evidence are valid. Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi mentioned that the validity of the claim is a condition for the validity of the settlement regarding denial. (Fath) He does not recoup anything: because his claim may be regarding what remains, even if little, so as long as something is in his hand, he does not recoup anything from him. (Ayni) He claimed all of it, i.e., all of the house, and he settled for one hundred, and then something of it was recovered. (Ayni) He recoups its portion: [i.e., his share of the settlement price. (T, Madan)] i.e., the portion of the recovered part; because the settlement for one hundred applied to the entire house, and when something of it is recovered, it becomes clear that the claimant does not own that amount, so he returns proportionally from the consideration. Its clarification: if he claimed a specific entire house and the settlement was for one hundred, and half the house was recovered, the defendant recoups fifty dirhams from the claimant. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/67] (Fath) Section: regarding the sale by an unauthorized person (fuduli). Al-fudul with damma on the fa is a plural of fadl, this plural is predominant in busying oneself with what does not concern him and in which he has no authority. In al-Mughrib: it is, in the terminology of the jurists, one who is not an agent, and opening the fa is an error. The better expression is: it is one who acts in the right of another without legal permission. (Fath) The property of another: without his order. It is suspended if the other is a mature sane person; otherwise, it is not concluded at all. (T) The owner may cancel it: absolutely, explicitly by saying 'I cancel', or implicitly by selling the object of contract to someone else, and silence after knowledge is not permission. Similarly, his statement: 'or he may ratify it' i.e., absolutely, explicitly by saying 'I have ratified', or implicitly by receiving the price from the buyer, or requesting it, or gifting it to the buyer, or giving it in charity to him. And his statement: 'if the two contractors remain etc.' is a condition for ratification; because ratification is a disposition regarding the contract, so its existence is necessary, and that is due to the existence of things. The principle in it is: every disposition issued by an unauthorized person while there is a ratifier at the time of its occurrence is concluded suspended upon ratification according to us, and it does not occur as void. But if there is no ratifier, it is not suspended and occurs void. Al-Shafi'i said: All dispositions of the unauthorized person are void and are not suspended upon ratification, because they issue without legal authority. And our proof: what is narrated from the hadith of Urwa ibn Abi al-Ja'd al-Bariqi that the Prophet gave him two dinars to buy a sheep for him, so he bought two sheep with them, sold one of them and came with a dinar and a sheep, and the Prophet prayed for blessing in his sale, so that if he had bought dust he would have profited in it. Narrated by Bukhari, Ahmad, and Abu Dawud. And the hadith of Habib ibn Abi Thabit from Hakim ibn Hizam that Ali sent him to buy a sacrificial animal for him with a dinar, so he bought a sacrificial animal and profited in it a dinar, so he bought another in its place and came with the sacrificial animal and the dinar to the Messenger of Allah, and if the sale had not been concluded... the Prophet would not have ratified it, and he would have rejected it. And because the pillar of the disposition issued from its people, added to its place, and there is no harm in its being concluded suspended, so it is concluded; because eligibility is by intellect and suitability by the property being valuable. And there is no harm to the owner because he has the choice to cancel or enforce; rather there is benefit for him in that the burden of seeking the buyer is removed from him, and benefit for the two contractors in preserving their speech from nullification. If you say: he does not own it, as he prohibited selling the commodity before taking possession and selling the runaway slave? I say: the discussion is about the conclusion of the contract, i.e., permissibility and impermissibility. The sale of the commodity before taking possession is concluded according to us, even if it is corrupt, and similarly the runaway slave in one narration, such that if he delivers it, it is not binding on us. The meaning of the hadith is that he should not sell something he does not own, then buy it and deliver it. If you say: this is gharar (deception), and he prohibited selling gharar as narrated by Muslim. I say: we do not concede that it is gharar; because it is suspended upon the owner's ratification. And the continued existence of the price is not required for the validity of ratification, except if it is a specific commodity, then its continued existence is also required. And when the owner ratifies, the price becomes owned by him as a trust in the hand of the unauthorized person, like an agent, so that he is not liable for its destruction in his hand, whether it is destroyed after ratification or before, because subsequent ratification is like prior agency. And the unauthorized person may cancel the contract before ratification to ward off rights from himself, and similarly the buyer may cancel the sale before ratification to avoid the binding of the contract. (Ayni, Fath) And 'lahu' meaning the owner remains. (Mahshi) And 'bihi' meaning the price if it is a specific commodity: i.e., goods that are specified by designation, so it becomes like the sold item. (Mahshi) And the statement 'the freeing by the buyer is valid' etc.: i.e., if he usurped a slave, and the buyer freed him, then the owner ratified the sale of the usurper, the freeing is valid from the buyer by istihsan according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). According to Muhammad and Zufar: it is not valid, and that is the analogy; because there is no freeing without ownership due to his statement 'There is no freeing in what a son of Adam does not own'. And the suspended ownership, even if it confers ownership, is retroactive, and it is established from one aspect but not from another, so it is not suitable as a condition for freeing; because the corrector for it is complete ownership for the hadith. And for them (the two shaykhs): ownership is established suspended by an absolute disposition intended to confer ownership, so the freeing is suspended upon it, and it becomes effective upon its effectiveness, and it is like the freeing by the buyer from the pledgor, for it is suspended and becomes effective by the ratification of the pledgee of the sale; because the principle is the connection of the ruling to the cause, and the delay is to ward off harm from the owner, and the harm is in the effectiveness of ownership, not in its suspension. And we do not concede that freeing needs ownership at the time of its establishment, but at the time of its effectiveness, and the meaning of the hadith is effective freeing. (Ayni, Fath) By ratification: i.e., by the owner's ratification, not his sale: i.e., the sale by the buyer from the usurper is not valid even if the owner ratifies his sale; because through ratification, complete ownership is established for the first buyer, who is the second seller, and when it attaches to the suspended ownership of another, it invalidates it due to the impossibility of combining complete ownership and suspended ownership in one place. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/68] (Fath) So he ratified: i.e., the sale, then the owner ratified the sale of the usurper.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary from Fath al-Qadir covers multiple rulings related to the sale of another's property (bay' al-fuduli). It begins with a ruling on a seller who sells another's property: he does not recoup anything from the owner if the claim is partial, but if he claimed the entire property and settled, he recoups proportionally if part is recovered. This is explained with an example: if a person claims a house, settles for 100, and half is later proved to belong to another, the defendant recoups 50. The passage then defines 'fuduli' as one who acts in another's right without legal permission. The core ruling is that such a sale is suspended on the owner's ratification if the owner is a sane adult; otherwise, it is void. The owner may cancel explicitly or implicitly, or ratify explicitly or implicitly (e.g., by receiving the price). Ratification requires the continued existence of the two contractors (seller and buyer) and the sold item, and also the price if it is a specific commodity. The commentary then presents the Shafi'i opposition: all fuduli dispositions are void. The Hanafi respond with two hadiths: the hadith of Urwa ibn Abi al-Ja'd, where the Prophet approved of his buying two sheep with one dinar and selling one, and the hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam acting as an agent for Ali. These show that the Prophet did not reject such transactions. The Hanafi argue that the pillar of the contract is validly issued (by a sane person concerning valuable property), and there is no harm to the owner who has the choice. They respond to objections about gharar (deception) by noting that the sale is suspended and not immediately binding. The passage then discusses a sub-case: if a usurper sells a slave, and the buyer frees him, then the owner ratifies the sale, does the freeing count? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs), it does, by istihsan, because ownership is established retroactively. According to Muhammad and Zufar, it does not, because complete ownership is needed at the time of freeing. The relied-upon position is the first, as it protects the act of freeing. Finally, the commentary notes that if the buyer re-sells the slave to another, that second sale is invalid even if the owner ratifies the first, because the first sale gave complete ownership that invalidates the suspended ownership of the second sale.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): One who acts in another's right without legal permission (not an agent, not a guardian).
موقوف (mawqūf): Suspended; a contract that is valid but whose effectiveness depends on a later event (ratification).
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification; the owner's consent that makes the suspended contract effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a sale made by a fuduli (unauthorized person) if the owner later ratifies it?
A) The sale is void and cannot become valid.
B) The sale was suspended and becomes valid upon ratification.
C) The sale is valid only if the price was paid.
D) The sale is null and void regardless of ratification.

Key Takeaway
A sale of another's property is not automatically void in the Hanafi school; it is a suspended contract that the owner may cancel or ratify, with strong hadith evidence supporting this view.

Ruling on Sale by a Non-Owner (Fuduli)
Establishes validity of fuduli sale as suspended upon ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a fuduli sale, distinguish it from void contracts, and apply the condition of ratification to determine enforceability.

Core Idea
A sale by a non-owner is valid but suspended; ratification makes it binding from the start.

In this lesson, we examine a fundamental Hanafi principle: a contract concluded by an unauthorized person (fuduli) is not automatically void. Instead, it is a valid but suspended contract, dependent on the owner's ratification. This ruling reflects the Hanafi emphasis on the substance of the contract over the form, and it is justified by the absence of harm and the benefit to all parties. We will explore the proofs, the objections, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its nature
What is the legal status of a sale by a non-owner? The Hanafi school rules it as valid but suspended (mawquf), not void.
2
Understand the rationale
Why is it considered valid? The pillars of the contract are present (qualified parties, valuable subject matter), and there is no harm to the owner who can choose to ratify or reject.
The owner benefits from being spared the trouble of finding a buyer.
3
Analyze the objections
What are the two main objections? Selling what one does not own (prohibited by hadith) and gharar (deception). The text presents these objections and then responds.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
How does the author refute the objections? The sale is not gharar because it depends on ratification; the hadith prohibition is interpreted narrowly to apply to sales where delivery is impossible or absolute inability.
The fuduli sale is not inherently deceptive.
5
Note the practical consequences
Who can rescind and when? Both fuduli and buyer may rescind before ratification. After ratification, the price becomes a trust like an agent's, and the owner assumes ownership of the price.
Conditions: parties and subject matter must still exist; if price is a specific commodity, it must also remain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع
حكم من باع ملك غيره
أو يجيزه إن بقي العاقدان والمعقود عليه وله وبه لو كان عرضاً. ولأن ركن التصرف صدر من أهله مضافاً إلى محله ولا ضرر في انعقاده موقوفا فينعقد؛ لأن الأهلية بالعقل والمحلية بكون المال متقوما، وليس فيه ضرر على المالك؛ لأنه مخير في الفسخ والنفاذ، بل له فيه منفعة حيث سقط عنه مؤنة طلب المشتري ونفع للمتعاقدين بصون كلامهما عن الإلغاء. فإن قلت: ما لا يملكه كما نهى عن بيع المبيع قبل القبض وعن بيع الأبق؟ قلت: الكلام في انعقاد العقد أي الجواز وعدمه، وبيع المبيع قبل القبض ينعقد عندنا وإن كان فاسدا، وكذا الآبق في رواية، حتى لو سلّمه فلا يلزمنا، والمراد من الحديث أن صح يبيع شيئاً لا يملكه ثم يشتريه ويسلمه. فإن قلت: هذا غرر ونهى عليه عن بيع الغرر فيما رواه مسلم، قلت: لا نسلم أنه غرر؛ لأنه يتوقف على إجازة المالك، ولا يشترط بقاء الثمن لصحة الإجازة، إلا إذا كان عرضا فحينئذ يشترط بقاؤها أيضاً، وإذا أجازه المالك كان الثمن مملوكا له أمانة في يد الفضولي بمنزلة الوكيل حتى لا يضمن بالهلاك في يده، سواء هلك بعد الإجازة أو قبلها؛ لأن الإجازة اللاحقة كالوكالة السابقة وللفضولي أن يفسخ العقد قبل الإجازة دفعا للحقوق عن نفسه، وكذا للمشتري فسخ البيع قبل الإجازة تحرزا عن لزوم العقد. (عيني، فتح) وله: أي بقي المعقود له وهو المالك. (محشي) وبه أي والمعقود به وهو الثمن عرضا: أي متاعاً يتعين بالتعيين فصار كالمبيع. (محشي)
The Book of Sales. The ruling on one who sells the property of another. Or it is ratified if the two contracting parties and the subject matter remain, and also the price if it was a specific commodity. Because the pillar of the transaction issued from one qualified to it, directed to its proper place, and there is no harm in its being concluded as suspended, so it is concluded; because qualification is by intellect and subject-matter by being valuable property, and there is no harm to the owner because he has the choice to rescind or enforce; rather he benefits from being spared the trouble of seeking a buyer, and there is benefit for the transactors in saving their words from being void. If you say: 'He does not own it, as the prohibition of selling before taking possession and selling a runaway slave?' I say: The discussion is about the validity of the contract i.e. its permissibility or not. Selling before possession is valid in our school though defective, and similarly the runaway slave in one narration, even if he delivers it he is not liable. The meaning of the hadith is that he sells something he does not own then buys it and delivers it. If you say: 'This is gharar and the Prophet forbade gharar as reported by Muslim?' I say: We do not accept it is gharar because it depends on the owner's ratification; and the survival of the price is not a condition for the validity of ratification, except if it is a specific commodity, then its survival is required. And when the owner ratifies, the price becomes owned by him as a trust in the fuduli's hand like an agent, so he is not liable for its destruction in his hand, whether it perishes after ratification or before; because subsequent ratification is like prior agency. The fuduli may rescind the contract before ratification to protect himself from liability, and likewise the buyer may rescind the sale before ratification to avoid the contract becoming binding. (al-Ayni, Fath) 'Walahu' means the person to whom the contract is made, i.e. the owner. (Mahashi) 'Wabihi' means the subject matter, i.e. the price, if it is a specific commodity, i.e., a good identified by specification, so it becomes like the sold object. (Mahashi)
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the sale by a non-owner (bay' al-fuduli). It begins by stating that such a sale is valid as suspended (mawquf), contingent upon ratification (ijaza) by the owner, provided the contracting parties (the fuduli and the buyer) and the subject matter (the sold item and the price, if a specific commodity) still exist. The commentator then explains the rationale: the essential pillars of the contract are present—qualified parties and a valid subject matter—and there is no harm in suspending the contract until ratification. The owner gains benefit by being spared the trouble of finding a buyer. The commentator addresses two objections: first, the Prophetic prohibition of selling what one does not own (selling before taking possession, selling runaway slaves). He clarifies that in the Hanafi school, such contracts do occur (they are valid but defective), and the hadith refers to selling something one cannot deliver. Second, the objection of gharar (deception) is rejected because the suspended contract depends on the owner's ratification. The price becomes a trust (amanah) in the hands of the fuduli, akin to an agent. The fuduli and the buyer may rescind before ratification. The commentary also specifies conditions for the price: if it is a specific commodity (ayn), it must still exist at ratification. This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who acts without authorization, specifically one who sells another's property without permission.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification or approval by the owner that makes the suspended sale effective.
غرر (gharar): Deception or uncertainty in a contract, prohibited in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal status of a sale made by a fuduli (non-owner) before ratification by the owner according to the Hanafi school?
A) Void (batil) from the outset
B) Valid but suspended (mawquf) and becomes binding upon ratification
C) Permissible but makruh
D) Invalid unless the owner repeats the contract

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a fuduli sale as a suspended contract, conditional on the owner's ratification, reflecting a pragmatic approach that avoids voiding potentially beneficial transactions.

Manumission by Buyer of Usurped Slave After Ratification
Intra-Hanafi dispute on manumission by buyer of usurped slave after ratification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of manumission by the buyer of a usurped slave after ratification and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Ratification can make manumission valid retroactively, but ownership conflict bars a second sale.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. When a buyer purchases a slave from a usurper and then manumits the slave before the owner ratifies the sale, does the manumission become valid after ratification? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say yes by istihsan; Muhammad and Zufar say no by qiyas. Understanding this dispute deepens our grasp of how ownership and ratification interact in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The buyer of a usurped slave manumits the slave after purchase but before ratification. Determine the legal act (itq) and the timing relative to ratification.
The manumission occurs while ownership is still in suspense.
2
State the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the manumission is valid upon ratification by istihsan. Ratification relates back, making ownership complete from the time of sale.
Istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.
3
State the opposing position
Muhammad al-Shaybani and Zufar hold that the manumission is invalid, relying on qiyas and the hadith 'La itq fi-ma la yamlik' – you cannot free what you do not own.
They require complete ownership at the moment of the act.
4
Trace the reasoning of each side
For Abu Hanifa: retroactive ownership suffices because the manumission takes effect only after ratification. For Muhammad: ownership must be present at the time of freeing; suspended ownership is insufficient.
This difference hinges on the time of effectiveness.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the slave is freed and then ratification occurs, in the first view the slave is free; in the second, he remains owned by the original owner.
The consequence affects the slave's status and the buyer's liability.
6
Identify the mufta bihi
The view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is the operative position in the Hanafi school, as relied upon by later authorities.
This is the ruling applied in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح عتق مشتر من الثمن عبدا غاصب بإجازة بيعه لا بيعه، ولو قطعت يده عند المشتري، فأخذ أرشه، فأجيز. وبإجازة: أي بإجازة المالك لا بيعه: أي لا يصح بيع المشتري من الغاصب وإن أجاز المولى بيعه؛ لأنه بالإجازة ثبت للمشتري الأول، وهو البائع الثاني ملك بات فإذا طرء على ملك موقوف لغيره أبطله؛ لاستحالة اجتماع الملك البات والملك الموقوف في محل واحد. (فتح) فأجيز : أي البيع ثم أجاز المالك البيع. بيع الغاصب.
And the manumission by the buyer of a slave [bought] from a usurper with the ratification of the sale is valid, not his sale [of the slave]. And if his hand is cut off while with the buyer, and he takes its compensation (arsh), and then the sale is ratified. 'With ratification' means with the ratification of the owner, not his sale: meaning the sale by the buyer (to a third party) from the usurper is not valid even if the owner ratifies the [original] sale; because by ratification the first buyer (who is the second seller) obtains complete ownership, and when complete ownership meets a suspended ownership for another, it invalidates it due to the impossibility of the coexistence of complete and suspended ownership in one subject. (Fath) 'Fujiza' means the sale is ratified, then the owner ratified the sale. The sale of the usurper.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of the owner's ratification on a slave bought from a usurper (ghasib). The main subject is the validity of manumission (itq) by the buyer after ratification. According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the manumission is valid by istihsan: although the buyer did not own the slave at the time of purchase, after ratification, ownership is deemed to have existed from the sale, so the manumission takes effect. Muhammad and Zufar disagree, relying on qiyas: the hadith 'La itq fi-ma la yamlik' (no manumission in what one does not own) indicates that a slave must be fully owned at the time of manumission; suspended ownership is insufficient. The commentary then clarifies that the buyer's subsequent sale of the slave to another is not valid even with ratification, because the buyer obtained complete ownership upon ratification, and that would conflict with the suspended ownership of the second buyer. The text also mentions the case where the slave's hand is cut and the buyer takes arsh; upon ratification, the arsh belongs to the buyer. This ruling is from the Mukhtasar al-Quduri and the commentary of al-Ayni and Fath.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Manumission or freeing of a slave.
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper, one who takes property without right.
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification, approval.
ملك بات (milk bat): Complete/unconditional ownership.
ملك موقوف (milk mawquf): Suspended ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, what is the status of a slave's manumission by the buyer after the owner ratifies the sale by the usurper?
A) Invalid, because the buyer did not own the slave at the time of purchase
B) Valid, because ratification establishes ownership retroactively
C) Valid only if the buyer also pays the full price
D) Invalid, because the usurper's sale is void

Key Takeaway
The manumission by the buyer of a usurped slave is valid upon ratification by istihsan, but the buyer's subsequent sale is not, due to the impossibility of coexisting ownership types.

Evidence Rejected Due to Contradiction in Fuduli Sale
Ruling on arsh and evidence in fuduli sale without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the buyer's evidence of non-authorization in a fuduli sale, and apply the principle of tanāquḍ to similar cases.

Core Idea
The buyer's own purchase contradicts a later claim of no authorization — evidence is rejected due to tanāquḍ.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a common situation: a person buys a slave from a third party (fuduli) and later discovers the seller had no permission. The buyer wants to rescind the sale by providing evidence of the seller's admission. Hanafi fiqh examines whether such evidence can be accepted. The ruling turns on the principle of legal contradiction (tanāquḍ): the buyer's own act of purchasing implies he believed the seller had authority, so his later claim to the contrary is inconsistent and his evidence is rejected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Ruling
The act is a fuduli sale: a person sells a slave belonging to another without permission. The buyer later claims the seller had no authorization and provides evidence (bayyinah) of the seller's admission. The ruling: the buyer's evidence is not accepted.
Also note the initial statement about arsh: compensation for injury to the slave belongs to the buyer, with any excess over half the price given in charity.
2
Locate the Legal Basis (Tanāquḍ)
The reason is tanāquḍ (contradiction). The buyer's act of purchasing implies that he considered the seller authorized. Claiming no authorization afterward contradicts his own prior conduct. Since the claim is baseless, evidence supporting it is also rejected.
The principle: 'al-ākhidh bi-al-bay' yadullu 'alā al-idhn' (taking by purchase indicates permission).
3
Note the Exception from al-Ziyadat
An exception: if the true owner (mustahiqq) claims the slave and the buyer confirms, then the buyer's evidence of the seller's admission that the slave belongs to the owner is accepted to recover the price. The difference is that here the slave is in the buyer's hand, while in al-Ziyadat it is in the owner's hand, making the sale not 'safe' for the buyer.
This highlights the condition of 'safety' (salāmah) of the sold item for the buyer.
4
Evaluate the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practically, the buyer cannot rescind the sale based solely on his own evidence. He must wait for the true owner to claim. If the owner does claim, the buyer can then use evidence to recover the price from the seller.
This ruling is mukhtalaf fih; other schools may accept the buyer's evidence in such cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع حكم من باع ملك غيره فأرشه المشتريه، وتصدق بما زاد على نصف الثمن. ولو باع عبد غيره بغير أمره، فبرهن المشتري على إقرار البائع، أو رب العبد أنه لم يأمره بالبيع، وأراد ردّ البيع، لم تقبل بيّنته، وإن أقر البائع بذلك عند القاضي لم تقبل بينته؛ لبطلان دعواه بالتناقض؛ إذ الإقدام على الشراء والبيع دليل على دعوى الصحة ونفاذ العقد؛ لأن الظاهر من حال العاقل المسلم مباشرة العقد الصحيح النافذ، ودليل على أن البائع يملك البيع، ودعوى الإقرار بعدم الأمر يناقضه، وقبول البينة يبتني على صحة الدعوى. فإذا بطلت الدعوى لا تقبل البينة، ولو لم يكن بينة، لكان القول لمدعي الأمر؛ إذ غيره متناقض، فلا تصح دعواه، ولهذا لم يكن له استحلافه ولا ينافيه ما في "الزيادات" من أن المشتري لو صدق المستحق على دعواه ثم برهن على إقرار البائع بأنه للمستحق ليرجع بالثمن يقبل؛ لأنه فرق بينهما بأن المبيع هنا في يد المشتري، فيكون المبيع سالماً له، فلا يثبت له حق الرجوع، وفيما في "الزيادات" في يد المستحق، فلا يكون المبيع سالماً للمشتري، فيثبت له حق الرجوع لوجدان شرطه. (عيني وفتح) أو رب العبد: أي وبرهن المشتري على إقرار رب العبد يعني مالكه. (عيني مسكين) بينته: أي بيئة المشتري؛ لبطلان دعواه بالتناقض. (عيني، معدن)
وإن أقر البائع بذلك: أي لو أقر البائع الفضولي بأن رب العبد لم يأمرني وقوله : "عند القاضي" ليس بقيد؛ لأن إقراره عند القاضي وغيره سواء، إلا أن البينة تختص بمجلس القاضي، فلهذا قال: عند القاضي. (فتح)
The Book of Sales: Ruling on one who sells the property of another: its compensation (arsh) belongs to the buyer, and he gives in charity what exceeds half the price. And if he sold the slave of another without his permission, and the buyer provides evidence of the seller's admission or the owner's admission that he did not authorize the sale, and he wants to rescind the sale, his evidence is not accepted. And if the seller admits that before the judge, his evidence is not accepted because his claim is invalidated by contradiction; since proceeding with the purchase and sale is evidence of the claim of validity and enforcement of the contract; because the apparent state of a rational Muslim is that he engages in a valid and effective contract, and it is evidence that the seller owned the sale, and the claim of admission of no authorization contradicts it, and acceptance of evidence is based on the validity of the claim. If the claim is invalid, the evidence is not accepted. If there was no evidence, the statement would be of the one who claims authorization; because the other is contradictory, so his claim is not valid, and for this he does not have the right to demand an oath. This does not contradict what is in al-Ziyadat that if the buyer confirms the claimant's claim then provides evidence of the seller's admission that the slave belongs to the claimant in order to recover the price, it is accepted; because the difference is that here the sold item is in the buyer's hand, so the sold item is safe for him, so he does not have the right of recourse, while in al-Ziyadat it is in the hand of the claimant, so the sold item is not safe for the buyer, so he has the right of recourse because his condition is met. (al-Ayni and Fath) 'Or the owner of the slave' means the buyer provides evidence of the admission of the owner of the slave, i.e., its owner. (al-Ayni, Miskin) 'His evidence' means the buyer's evidence; because his claim is invalidated by contradiction. (al-Ayni, Ma'din)
And if the seller admits that, meaning if the fuduli seller admits that the owner did not order me, and his saying 'before the judge' is not a condition; because his admission before the judge and elsewhere is the same, except that evidence is specific to the court, so he said 'before the judge'. (Fath)
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a specific scenario: a fuduli sells a slave without the owner's permission, and the buyer later discovers that the seller had no permission. The buyer tries to rescind the sale by providing evidence that the seller or the owner admitted there was no authorization. According to the text, such evidence is not accepted because the buyer's own act of purchasing contradicts his claim—by buying, he implicitly affirmed that the seller had authority. Accepting evidence would be inconsistent. However, an exception from al-Ziyadat is noted: if the rightful owner (mustahiqq) claims the slave and the buyer confirms, then provides evidence of the seller's admission that the slave belonged to the claimant, the evidence is accepted to recover the price, because the slave is in the hand of the claimant, not the buyer. The detailed commentary distinguishes the two cases. The arsh (compensation for injury) belongs to the buyer, and any amount exceeding half the price must be given in charity, because the buyer's ownership was incomplete at the time of injury. This ruling is from the Mukhtasar al-Quduri and the commentaries of al-Ayni and Fath.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Compensation paid for injury to a slave, analogous to diyah for a free person.
تناقض (tanāquḍ): Legal inconsistency where a party's actions contradict their claim, leading to rejection of the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Buyu — Sales and Trade

15 min
Ruling on Selling Another's Property Without Permission (Fuduli Sale)
Ruling on sale by fuduli and proof of lack of permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal consequences of a fuduli sale, including the buyer's entitlement to compensation, the obligation to give charity on excess, the effect of contradiction on evidence, and the intra-Hanafi dispute on the owner's right to demand the price.

Core Idea
A fuduli sale is suspended; the buyer receives arsh but gives sadaqah on excess over half price; contradictory evidence is rejected.

This lesson unpacks the complex rulings of a fuduli sale—a sale by a non-owner. You will see how the buyer's ownership is incomplete at the time of injury, leading to a rule requiring charity on any compensation exceeding half the price. You will also learn the critical principle that a buyer's own contradictory claims may bar his evidence. Finally, we examine a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn regarding the owner's recourse.

Guided Path
1
State the Basic Rule for Arsh and Sadaqah
Read the passage's opening: the buyer gets arsh (compensation) for injury to the slave and must give in charity any amount exceeding half the price he paid. Identify these two components.
The arsh is for the buyer because ownership is complete upon ratification, but the excess over half is not fully his at the time of injury.
2
Understand the Legal Rationale for the Sadaqah Rule
Why only half? At the time of injury, the buyer's ownership is suspended. He has paid the full price but only half the value is under his guarantee (the other half belongs to the owner pending ratification). Thus, he cannot retain the full compensation.
This is based on the idea that the sale is mawquf; the buyer's risk is only half the price before ratification.
3
Analyze the Contradiction Principle for Evidence
The passage states that if the buyer proves the seller confessed before the sale that he had no permission, his evidence is rejected. Why? Because his act of buying implies he believed the seller had authority. Claiming otherwise contradicts his own action.
This is a key fiqh principle: one cannot act on a basis and then deny that basis to gain an advantage.
4
Distinguish Pre-Sale vs. Post-Sale Confession
If the seller confesses after the sale that he had no permission, the buyer's evidence is accepted—there is no contradiction because the confession does not conflict with the buyer's previous belief. Understand the difference.
The timing is crucial: pre-sale confession challenges the validity of the contract from its inception; post-sale confession does not.
5
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Owner's Recourse
When the seller confesses no permission and the owner denies, the owner can demand the price from the seller according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf alone holds that the owner can also demand directly from the buyer. Compare the proofs and practical consequences.
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view: the owner's recourse is only against the seller.
