فإذا بطلت الدعوى لا تقبل البينة، ولو لم يكن بينة، لكان القول لمدعي الأمر؛ إذ غيره متناقض، فلا تصح دعواه، ولهذا لم يكن له استحلافه ولا ينافيه ما في "الزيادات" من أن المشتري لو صدق المستحق على دعواه ثم برهن على إقرار البائع بأنه للمستحق ليرجع بالثمن يقبل؛ لأنه فرق بينهما بأن المبيع هنا في يد المشتري، فيكون المبيع سالماً له، فلا يثبت له حق الرجوع، وفيما في "الزيادات" في يد المستحق، فلا يكون المبيع سالماً للمشتري، فيثبت له حق الرجوع لوجدان شرطه. عيني وفتح) أو رب العبد: أي وبرهن المشتري على إقرار رب العبد يعني مالكه. (عيني مسكين) بينته: أي بيئة المشتري؛ لبطلان دعواه بالتناقض. (عيني، معدن)
وإن أقر البائع بذلك: أي لو أقر البائع الفضولي بأن رب العبد لم يأمرني وقوله : "عند القاضي" ليس بقيد؛

حكم من باع ملك غيره
كتاب البيوع بطل البيعُ إن طلب المشتري ذلك، ومن باع دار غيره، وأدخلها المشتري في بنائه
لم يضمن البائع. صحيح قيمة الدار أي بطلان البيع ونقضه أي عرصته بغير أمره
إن طلب المشتري ذلك شرط لبطلان البيع أي إذا أقر البائع بأن رب العبد لم يأمره بالبيع، وطلب المشتري نقض البيع بطل البيع؛ لأن التناقض لا يمنع صحة الإقرار؛ لعدم التهمة، فللمشتري أن يساعده على ذلك، فيتحقق الاتفاق بينهما، فيبطل البيع في حقهما لا في حق رب العبد إن كذبهما، وادعى أنه كان بأمره، فإذا لم ينفسخ في حقه يطالب البائع بالثمن عندهما؛ لأنه وكيله، وليس له أن يطالب المشتري؛ لأنه برئ بالتصادق، وعند أبي يوسف له أن يطالبه، فإذا أدى رجع به على البائع، بناء على أن إبراء الوكيل المشتري من الثمن عندهما، ويضمن للموكل، وعنده: لا يصح. ولو كان بالعكس بأن أنكر المالك التوكيل وتصادقا أنه وكله، فإن أقام الوكيل البينة لزمه، وإلا استحلف المالك، فإن حلف لم يلزمه، وإن نكل لزمه؛ لأن النكول كالإقرار ولو غاب المالك بعد الإنكار، وطلب البائع الفسخ، فسخ القاضي البيع بينهما؛ لأنه ثبت عند القاضي أن البيع كان موقوفاً، فإن طلب المشتري تأخير الفسخ ليحلف المالك على أنه لم يأمره يؤخر؛ لأن سبب الفسخ قد تحقق فلا يجوز تأخيره لأجل اليمين، فلو حضر المالك وحلف أخذ العبد، وإن نكل عاد البيع ولو كان المالك حاضرا وغاب المشتري لم يأخذ البائع؛ لأن العبد صبح ظاهراً بيع القضاء على الغائب بفسخه وللبائع أن يحلف رب العبد أنه ما أمره ببيعه، فإذا نكل ثبت أمره، وإن حلف ضمن البائع ونفذ بيعه؛ لأن في المضمونات يثبت الملك مستندا. (عيني، فتح، غيره)
The Book of Sales. The ruling on one who sells the property of another: The buyer gets the compensation for the cut limb, and he gives in charity what exceeds half the price. If someone sells another's slave without his permission, the buyer [gets the compensation] because he did not enter into his liability. If the buyer proves the seller's confession, or the owner's confession that he did not authorize the sale, and he wants to annul the sale, i.e., he brings the evidence. [If] the intermediary's evidence [is brought regarding the seller's confession before the sale], his evidence is not accepted. If the seller confesses that before the judge, the buyer [benefits]. The intermediary seller [is mentioned]. The owner of the slave [is the seller]. The sale of the mentioned slave [is discussed]. So his compensation is for the buyer: the pronoun in 'his compensation' refers to the cut, and it is not permissible for the pronoun to refer to the hand due to the required feminine because it is feminine by usage. What is meant by cutting the hand is inflicting a wound that necessitates compensation, and this compensation is for the buyer because ownership was complete for him from the time of purchase, so it becomes clear that the cutting occurred on his property, so the compensation is for him. And similarly, whatever occurs in the sold item of gain, offspring, or injury before ratification is for the buyer, contrary to if his hand was cut while with the usurper and then he guaranteed its value; the compensation is not for him because usurpation is not made a cause for ownership; it is only established by necessity. And his statement: 'and he gives in charity what exceeds half the price' because ownership did not exist in reality at the time of cutting, and the compensation for one hand in a free person is half the blood money, and in a slave is half the value. What entered into his liability is what is in exchange for the price, so what exceeds half the price has an element of non-ownership due to what we said (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 2/68) (Fath). 'Without his permission' qualifies the absence of permission, even though it occurs in al-Jami' al-Saghir, but it is not part of the scenario at all. And his statement: 'so the buyer proves' – similarly, if the seller proves the buyer's confession of no permission, his proof is accepted. This applies if the buyer brings evidence of the seller's confession before the sale. If he brings evidence of his confession after the sale, it is accepted due to lack of contradiction. And his statement: 'his evidence is not accepted' because his claim is invalidated by contradiction; since undertaking to buy and sell indicates a claim of validity and effectiveness of the contract because the apparent state of a rational Muslim is that he performs a valid, effective contract, and it indicates that the seller owns the sale, and claiming a confession of no permission contradicts that. Acceptance of evidence depends on the validity of the claim. So when the claim is invalidated, the evidence is not accepted. If there were no evidence, the statement would be that of the one claiming permission, since the other is contradictory, so his claim is not valid; hence he does not have the right to have him sworn. This is not contradicted by what is in al-Ziyadat, that if the buyer confirms the rightful claimant's claim and then proves the seller's confession that it belongs to the rightful claimant to recover the price, it is accepted. Because there is a difference: here the sold item is in the buyer's possession, so the sold item is safe for him, and he has no right of recourse. In what is in al-Ziyadat, it is in the possession of the rightful claimant, so the sold item is not safe for the buyer, and he has the right of recourse since its condition is found. ('Ayni wa Fath) 'Or the owner of the slave': i.e., the buyer proves the confession of the owner of the slave, meaning the owner. ('Ayni Miskin) 'His evidence': i.e., the buyer's evidence, due to the invalidity of his claim by contradiction. ('Ayni, Ma'din) And if the seller confesses that: i.e., if the intermediary seller confesses that the owner of the slave did not authorize me, and his statement 'before the judge' is not a restriction because his confession before the judge and elsewhere is the same, except that evidence is specific to the judge's court, so he said: before the judge. (Fath) Ruling on one who sells the property of another. The Book of Sales: The sale is invalid if the buyer demands that. And one who sells another's house and the buyer incorporates it into his building, the seller does not guarantee the value of the house. Correct [i.e., the invalidation of the sale and its annulment] its plot without permission. 'If the buyer demands that' is a condition for the invalidation of the sale, i.e., if the seller confesses that the owner of the slave did not authorize the sale, and the buyer demands to annul the sale, the sale is invalid because contradiction does not prevent the validity of the confession due to the absence of suspicion. So the buyer can assist him in that, and thus agreement is realized between them, and the sale is invalidated between them, not in the right of the owner if he denies them and claims that it was with his permission. Then if it is not annulled regarding him, he demands the price from the seller according to the two [Sahibayn] because he is his agent, and he cannot demand from the buyer because he is absolved by mutual confirmation. According to Abu Yusuf, he can demand from him; if he pays, he recovers from the seller, based on the fact that the agent's absolution of the buyer from the price is valid according to the two [Sahibayn], and he guarantees for the principal; according to him [Abu Yusuf], it is not valid. And if the opposite were the case: the owner denies the agency and they both confirm that he appointed him as agent, then if the agent brings evidence, it is binding; otherwise, the owner is sworn; if he swears, it is not binding; if he refuses, it is binding because refusal is like confession. And if the owner is absent after denial and the seller demands rescission, the judge rescinds the sale between them because it is established before the judge that the sale was suspended. If the buyer requests a postponement of rescission to have the owner swear that he did not authorize him, it is delayed because the cause of rescission has been realized, so it is not permissible to delay it for the sake of the oath. Then if the owner appears and swears, he takes the slave; if he refuses, the sale returns. And if the owner is present and the buyer is absent, the seller does not take it because the slave [is considered] apparently [owned by the buyer?] and it is not permissible to pass judgment against the absent person by rescinding it. And the seller may swear the owner of the slave that he did not authorize his sale; if he refuses, his authority is established; if he swears, the seller is liable and his sale becomes effective because in liability matters ownership is established retrospectively. ('Ayni, Fath, others)
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq expounds the ruling on a sale of another's property without permission (bay' al-fuduli). The key points:

1. If a third party cuts the limb of a slave sold by a fuduli, the buyer is entitled to the compensation (arsh) because ownership transfers retroactively upon ratification. The buyer also gives as sadaqah any amount exceeding half the price of the slave, because at the time of injury his ownership was incomplete (only half the value was under his guarantee, the other half being suspended).

2. If the buyer proves that the seller confessed before the sale that he had no permission, his evidence is not accepted due to contradiction: his act of buying implies he believed the seller had authority. Confession after the sale is accepted as it does not contradict his earlier action.

3. If the buyer proves the owner's confession of no permission (rather than the seller's), the same principle applies: the buyer's claim is contradictory if the owner's confession was before the sale.

4. If the seller himself confesses lack of permission, his confession is valid, and the buyer may demand rescission. The sale is then void between them but remains valid if the owner denies and claims he had authorized it. In that case, the owner may demand the price from the seller (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) but cannot demand from the buyer because the buyer is discharged by mutual agreement. Abu Yusuf differs: the owner can demand payment from the buyer, who then recovers from the seller.

5. If the owner sues the seller, and the seller claims agency but the owner denies, the seller must prove it; otherwise the owner is sworn. Refusal to swear (nukul) is tantamount to confession.

6. If the owner is absent, the judge may rescind the sale at the seller's request. The buyer cannot postpone rescission to await the owner's oath. If the owner later appears and swears he did not authorize, he takes the property; if he refuses, the sale is reinstated.

7. The seller may swear the owner that he did not authorize; if the owner refuses, the seller's authority is proven; if he swears, the seller becomes liable and the sale is effective retroactively.

The reasoning throughout is based on the principle of legal contradiction (tanaqud) and the presumption that a Muslim's actions are valid and effective.

Key Terms
الفضولي (al-fuduli): A person who sells the property of another without permission; the sale is suspended (mawquf) pending ratification.
الأرش (al-arsh): Compensation for injury to a slave (or free person) measured by value or fixed amount.
التناقض (al-tanaqud): Legal contradiction between one's action and claim; here, buying and then claiming seller lacked permission is contradictory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a buyer's evidence of the seller's confession (made before the sale) that he had no permission not accepted?
A) Because the seller's confession is unreliable
B) Because the buyer's act of buying implies he believed the seller had permission, so his claim is contradictory
C) Because the seller must confess in person before the judge
D) Because the owner must be the one to bring the claim

Key Takeaway
A fuduli sale creates suspended ownership; the buyer gets arsh but gives sadaqah on excess, and his contradictory claims invalidate his evidence.

Selling Another's House: Liability When Buyer Builds
Ruling on seller's liability with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the final Hanafi position holds that a seller of another's house is not liable for its value, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on ghasb of immovable property.

Core Idea
The seller is not liable because Hanafis do not recognize ghasb of immovable property, so the seller is not a usurper.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a scenario where someone sells another's house, the buyer builds on it, and the seller later admits it was usurped. The ruling might seem surprising: the seller is not liable for the house's value. Why? The commentary reveals an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether usurpation (ghasb) of immovable property is legally possible. Understanding this distinction clarifies the ruling and the Hanafi position on liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Read the passage: a person sells another's house, the buyer incorporates it into his building. The seller does not guarantee the value of the house. This is the stated ruling.
The seller confesses to usurpation, but the buyer denies. The owner must prove ownership.
2
Understand the procedural reason
The seller's confession does not bind the buyer; the owner must bring evidence. If the owner fails, the loss is attributed to his inability, not the seller's contract.
This is why the incorporation into building is irrelevant to liability.
3
Recognize the deeper legal principle
The underlying issue is whether ghasb (usurpation) of immovable property is legally recognized. Abu Hanifa and later Abu Yusuf say no; Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i say yes.
Hanafis restrict ghasb to movable property that can be physically taken.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf initially agreed with Muhammad that the seller is liable, but later retracted to Abu Hanifa's view. The final position is the later view of Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa.
This is a classic example of tarjih within the Hanafi school.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the final view, the seller is not liable for the land's value; the owner's recourse is against the buyer's building (which can be removed). Under Muhammad's view, the seller would pay the value.
The practical difference is significant in cases where the owner appears.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن باع دار غيره وأدخلها المشتري في بنائه لم يضمن البائع قيمة الدار. ومعنى المسألة: أن يعترف البائع بالغصب ويكذبه المشتري؛ لأن إقرار البائع لا يسري على المشتري، ولابد من البرهان فإذا لم يقم صاحب الدار البينة كان التلف مضافاً إلى عجزه عن إقامة البينة لا إلى عقد البائع؛ لأن الغاصب لا يجوز بيعه، ولهذا لا يضمن البائع قيمة الدار، وقوله: "وأدخلها المشتري في بنائه" وقع اتفاقاً؛ إذ لا تأثير للإدخال في البناء في عدم سراية قول البائع على المشتري أن ما بعته غصب، وهو قول أبي يوسف آخراً وقول الإمام أيضاً، وكان أبو يوسف يقول أولاً: يضمن البائع قيمتها، وهو قول محمد، مسألة غصب العقار أنه هل يتحقق أم لا؟ عند محمد وزفر والشافعي: يتحقق، وعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف: لا يتحقق. (عيني، فتح، مسكين)
And one who sells another's house and the buyer incorporates it into his building, the seller does not guarantee the value of the house. The meaning of the issue: that the seller confesses to usurpation while the buyer denies him, because the seller's confession does not affect the buyer, and proof is necessary. So if the owner of the house does not bring evidence, the loss is attributed to his inability to bring evidence, not to the seller's contract, because the usurper's sale is not permissible. Hence the seller does not guarantee the house's value. And his statement: 'and the buyer incorporates it into his building' occurs by chance, because the incorporation into building has no effect on the non-transference of the seller's statement to the buyer that what I sold was usurped. This is the later view of Abu Yusuf and also the view of the Imam [Abu Hanifa]. Abu Yusuf initially said: the seller guarantees its value, and that is the view of Muhammad. The issue of usurpation of immovable property: does it occur or not? According to Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i: it does occur. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: it does not occur. ('Ayni, Fath, Miskin)
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where someone sells another's house (without permission), and the buyer builds on it. The seller then admits it was usurped, but the buyer denies (i.e., claims he thought it was valid). The ruling is that the seller is not liable for the value of the house, even if the buyer builds on it and the owner later proves his ownership. The reason: the seller's confession of usurpation does not bind the buyer; the owner must bring evidence. If the owner fails to prove his ownership, the loss is due to his failure, not the seller's wrongful act. Moreover, the buyer's building is irrelevant; it does not change the liability. This is the final view of Abu Yusuf coinciding with Abu Hanifa. Initially, Abu Yusuf held that the seller would guarantee the house's value, which is also Muhammad's view. The underlying dispute is whether usurpation (ghasb) of immovable property (al-‘aqaar) is possible. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, ghasb of immovable is not possible because it cannot be physically taken; only constructive possession is possible. Thus, the seller is not a ghāṣib (usurper) in the full sense and thus not liable for the value. Muhammad and Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that ghasb of immovable is possible, so the seller would be liable.

Key Terms
الغصب (ghasb): Wrongful taking of another's property without right; includes usurpation of both movable and immovable property according to some, but Hanafis restrict it to movables.
العقار (al-'aqar): Immovable property such as land and buildings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the final Hanafi position, why does the seller not guarantee the value of the house when he sells another's land and the buyer builds on it?
A) Because the buyer is at fault for building without verifying ownership
B) Because ghasb (usurpation) of immovable property is not recognized, so the seller is not a usurper
C) Because the seller's confession is accepted over the buyer's denial
D) Because the owner must first claim the building, not the land

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's final position absolves the seller of liability for the land's value because immovable property cannot be usurped in the legal sense that triggers compensation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Selling Another's Property and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Guarantee
Details the ruling on selling another's property and the intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the sale of another's property is void when the seller admits lack of authorization and the buyer demands rescission, and to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding the seller's guarantee when the buyer builds on the property.

Core Idea
A sale of another's property is void if the seller admits no authorization and the buyer demands rescission; the seller's guarantee for improvements divides the school.

This passage from the chapter on sales addresses a common scenario: a person sells property that does not belong to him. The text explores what happens when the seller admits his lack of authority and the buyer seeks to rescind the contract. It then delves into an important intra-Hanafi dispute: if the buyer builds on the property, does the seller guarantee its value? The positions of the early Hanafi masters reveal a deeper disagreement about the nature of usurpation (ghasb) of real estate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
The passage states that if the seller admits the owner did not authorize the sale, and the buyer demands rescission, the sale becomes void between them but not in the owner's right. Understand the condition: the buyer's demand is essential.
The sale is void (batil) only if the buyer demands it; otherwise it remains suspended.
2
Understand the role of admission and mutual agreement
The seller's admission (iqrar) binds only himself, not the buyer. The buyer can assist the seller in confirming the lack of authorization, and their agreement makes the sale void in their own right. The owner can still sue the seller for the price if he denies.
Iqrar is a strong proof but does not affect third parties.
3
Recognize the building scenario
The passage considers a case where the buyer incorporates the purchased property into his building. The key question: does the seller guarantee the value of the original property? This leads to the intra-Hanafi dispute.
The building scenario is an incidental example; the principle applies to any improvement.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (final opinion) hold that the seller does not guarantee, while Muhammad and Abu Yusuf's earlier opinion hold that he does. Understand the rationale: the seller's admission does not extend to the buyer, so the buyer's claim is unsupported.
This dispute is linked to the underlying issue of ghasb of real estate.
5
Connect to the underlying dispute on ghasb of real estate
Those who consider ghasb of real estate possible (Muhammad, Zufar, al-Shafi'i) are more likely to impose guarantee, while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf deny ghasb of real estate and thus deny guarantee. Know the thamarah al-khilaf: practical consequence for the buyer.
The dispute on ghasb affects many rulings beyond this one.

Ruling on whoever sells another's property. The book of sales: the sale becomes void if the buyer demands that. And whoever sells another's house, and the buyer incorporates it into his building, the seller does not guarantee (its value). Correct. The value of the house. i.e., the annulment of the sale and its rescission. i.e., its plot without his permission. 'If the buyer demands that' is a condition for the annulment of the sale, i.e., when the seller admits that the master of the slave did not order him to sell, and the buyer demands rescission of the sale, the sale becomes void; because contradiction does not prevent the validity of the admission due to lack of suspicion. So the buyer may assist him in that, thus agreement between them is achieved, and the sale becomes void in their right but not in the right of the master of the slave if he denies them and claims that it was by his order. So if it is not rescinded in his right, he (the master) demands the price from the seller according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?) because he is his agent, and he cannot demand from the buyer because he is discharged by mutual confirmation. According to Abu Yusuf, he can demand him, and if he pays, he recovers from the seller, based on the fact that the agent's discharge of the buyer from the price is valid according to them, and he is liable to the principal, and according to him it is not valid. If the opposite occurs, i.e., the owner denies the agency and they both confirm that he authorized him, then if the agent produces evidence, it is binding; otherwise the owner is put on oath; if he swears, it is not binding; if he refuses (nukul), it is binding because refusal is like admission. If the owner is absent after denial and the seller demands rescission, the judge rescinds the sale between them because it is established before the judge that the sale was suspended. If the buyer asks to delay rescission to let the owner swear that he did not order him, it is delayed; because the cause of rescission has been established, so it is not permissible to delay it for the sake of the oath. Then if the owner appears and swears, he takes the slave; if he refuses, the sale reverts. If the owner is present and the buyer is absent, the seller does not take (the price?) because the slave is apparently... (unclear). The judge's sale against the absent party by its rescission. And the seller may swear the master of the slave that he did not order him to sell it. If he refuses, his order is established; if he swears, the seller is liable and his sale is executed because in cases of guarantee (madhmunat) ownership is established retrospectively. (from 'Ayni, Fath, others). 'And whoever sells another's house, etc.' The meaning of the issue: that the seller admits usurpation and the buyer denies him, because the seller's admission does not extend to the buyer, and proof is necessary. If the owner of the house does not produce evidence, the loss is attributed to his inability to produce evidence, not to the seller's contract, because the usurper's sale is not permissible. Hence the seller does not guarantee the value of the house. And his statement: 'and the buyer incorporates it into his building' occurred incidentally, because incorporation into the building has no effect on the non-extension of the seller's statement to the buyer that what I sold was usurped. This is the final opinion of Abu Yusuf and also of the Imam (Abu Hanifa). Abu Yusuf initially used to say: the seller guarantees its value, and that is the opinion of Muhammad. The issue of usurpation of real estate: whether it is actualized or not? According to Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i: it is actualized. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: it is not actualized. (from 'Ayni, Fath, Miskin).
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on sales (Kitab al-Buyu') deals with the ruling when a person sells property belonging to someone else without permission. The text is a commentary (sharh) from works like al-'Ayni and Fath al-Qadir. It begins by stating the basic ruling: if the seller admits that the owner did not authorize the sale, and the buyer demands rescission, the sale is void. The commentary explains that the buyer can assist the seller in confirming this, and the sale is void between them but not in the owner's right if he denies. Then it discusses various scenarios: when the owner is absent, when the owner denies agency but the seller and buyer confirm it, and when the buyer builds on the purchased land. An important intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: whether the seller guarantees the value if the buyer incorporates the property into his building. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (in his later opinion) say no guarantee; Muhammad and Abu Yusuf's earlier opinion say yes. The passage also addresses the general issue of whether usurpation (ghasb) of real estate can occur. According to Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i, it can; according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, it cannot (because real estate is not movable and thus not subject to the usual ghasb rules). The text uses terms like 'iqrar (admission), 'nukul' (refusal to take an oath), and 'tawkeel' (agency).

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Taking another's property without right, including usurpation of real estate.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession; here, the seller's admission of lack of authorization.
نكول (nukul): Refusal to take an oath, treated as an admission of the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the sale of another's property is void at the buyer's demand when the seller admits lack of authorization, and the seller does not guarantee the property's value if the buyer builds, following the final opinion of Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa.

Salam: Definition, Proof, and the Istihsan Override
Introduction to salam with proof and istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of salam, identify its Quranic and Sunnah proofs, and explain why the Hanafi school overrides qiyas using istihsan.

Core Idea
Salam is a concession from the prohibition of selling what you do not possess, proven by Quran and Sunnah despite qiyas.

In this lesson, we explore the unique contract of salam (advance sale). Unlike ordinary sales, salam requires payment upfront for a described item to be delivered later. We'll examine its definition, the primary proofs from the Quran and hadith, and the fascinating legal reasoning (istihsan) that overrides the apparent prohibition by analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is salam? It is a sale where the price is paid immediately for a deferred delivery of a described commodity. The ruling: permissible as a concession (rukhsa).
Hanafis distinguish between 'azima (strict ruling) and rukhsa (concession).
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites Quran 2:282 and the hadith of the Prophet allowing salam with known measure and weight.
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Quranic command to write deferred contracts includes salam. The hadith explicitly permits salam with conditions.
4
Understand the qiyas objection
Analogy (qiyas) would prohibit salam because the sold item is non-existent at contract time, similar to selling what one does not possess.
Qiyas would make salam prohibited.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response: istihsan
The Hanafis abandon the qiyas due to the explicit permission of the texts and the practical need. This is an application of istihsan.
The conditions (description, quantity, term) remove uncertainty.

Chapter on Salam: i.e., explaining its rulings. Whatever can have its description fixed and its quantity known, salam is valid in it; and what cannot, then not. So it is valid in measurable items, everything whose description is known, and weighable items that are fungible (al-muthamman), like honey and oil. Chapter on Salam: (with fatha on the lam) literally means haste, and legally it is a sale of a thing on the basis that it is a debt on the seller with valid conditions. Know that selling a specific item for a debt is a resolve ('azima), and selling a debt for a specific item is a concession (rukhsa). When he finished explaining the first, he started on the second, which is salam. This type of sale is distinguished by this name because of its specific ruling that indicates its literal meaning: the acceleration of one of the two compensations before the presence of the sold item. The sold item is called muslam fih, the price is ra's al-mal, the seller is muslam ilayh, and the buyer is rabb al-salam. It is legislated by the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the ummah. Ibn Abbas said: 'I bear witness that Allah permitted deferred salam.' And he recited the verse: 'O you who have believed, when you contract a debt for a specified term, write it down' (2:282). And we have narrated that he (the Prophet) forbade selling what is not with a person, but he granted a concession for salam. The reason, and analogy (qiyas) would prohibit its permissibility because the muslam fih is a sold item and it is non-existent, and selling an existing item that is not owned, or owned but not in possession for delivery, is not permissible, so selling a non-existent item is even more so. But we abandoned analogy for what we mentioned. And it is synonymous with 'salaf' linguistically, and from it are derived 'salam', 'islam', 'salaf', 'islaf'. Legally, it is a contract that establishes ownership of the price immediately and of the priced item (muslam fih) deferred. Some say: it is taking an immediate thing for a deferred thing. And it is not concluded with the word 'sale' (bay'). This is the view of Zufar, 'Isa ibn Aban, and al-Shafi'i in one opinion. In the narration of al-Hasan: it is concluded, and this is the more correct (asahh). Its pillars are offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). It is concluded with the word 'salam' and upon that the narrations agree. The more correct view is that it is also concluded with the word 'sale'. Its legislation is due to severe need. Its ruling: ownership of the price is established for the muslam ilayh, and for the rabb al-salam, ownership of the muslam fih as a debt in the dhimmah; as for the specific item (ayn), it is not established except by taking possession (qabd). (from 'Ayni, Fath). And knowing its quantity: like measurable, weighable, and length-measurable items. In some copies: 'knowing its measure.' (from 'Ayni) Salam is valid in it because it does not lead to dispute after description is fixed and quantity known. And the Prophet said: 'Whoever among you advances salam in dates, let him advance salam in known measure and known weight to a known term.' Reported by Bukhari and Muslim. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/70). 'And what cannot, then not': i.e., every thing in which description and quantity cannot be fixed, salam is not valid in it because it leads to dispute, like animals, jewels, and pearls. (from Ta', 'Ayni). 'al-muthamman' (fungible): and he restricted it with his saying 'al-muthamman' to exclude dirhams and dinars, for they are weighable but they are not fungible (muthamman) but rather are prices (athman) by nature, so salam is not permissible in them. If the capital (ra's al-mal) is also a price, the contract is void completely. And if it is other than the price, like a cloth for ten dirhams, it is not valid as salam by agreement. And is it concluded as a sale with a deferred price? Abu Bakr al-A'mash said: yes. 'Isa ibn Aban said: no, and this is the more correct, because the correction of the contract is only in the place that necessitated... (incomplete in original).. agreement.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Salam (advance sale) in the book of sales. It defines salam linguistically (haste) and legally (a sale where the price is paid immediately for a specific described good to be delivered later). The author notes that salam is a concession (rukhsa) from the general prohibition of selling what one does not possess, which is a strict ruling ('azima). The proof of its legitimacy is from the Quran (2:282) and the hadith of the Prophet allowing salam with known measure and weight. The text explains that qiyas would prohibit salam because the sold item is non-existent at the time of contract, but this qiyas is set aside by the revealed texts (istihsan). The conditions for validity are that the item must be describable and quantifiable (like measurable, weighable, or length-measurable items). Items that cannot be precisely described (like jewels) are excluded. The text also discusses the dispute over whether salam can be concluded using the word 'sale' (bay')—the preferred view is that it can, though some early Hanafis (Zufar, 'Isa ibn Aban) and al-Shafi'i say no. Additionally, salam is not valid in currencies (dinars and dirhams) because they are prices themselves, not commodities. Finally, if a salam contract is formed but the conditions are not met, it may be considered a regular sale with deferred payment, though there is disagreement on this.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A sale where the price is paid in advance for a specified deferred delivery of a described commodity.
رخصة (rukhsa): A concession or dispensation from a general ruling due to necessity.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to derive rulings.
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference that abandons qiyas in favor of a stronger evidence or need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary method used by Hanafis to override the qiyas that would prohibit salam?
A) Ijma'
B) Qiyas jali
C) Istihsan
D) Istislah

Key Takeaway
Salam is a valid contract by Quran and Sunnah, and the Hanafi school uses istihsan to override the apparent qiyas prohibition.

Conditions for a Valid Salam Contract and the Dispute on the Word 'Sale'
Conditions for valid salam and disputes on wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the conditions for a valid salam contract, identify which items are eligible, and resolve the intra-Hanafi dispute on using the word 'sale'.

Core Idea
Salam requires the subject matter to be precisely described and quantifiable; jewels and currencies are excluded; the contract can be concluded with the word 'sale' according to the preferred view.

Salam is not a free-for-all; it has strict conditions to prevent disputes. The subject (muslam fih) must be something whose description can be fixed and quantity known—like grains, oils, and other fungibles. Additionally, the contract cannot be formed using currencies as the subject, and there is a dispute whether the contract can be concluded with the word 'sale'. We'll examine these conditions and the preferred ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions
The text states: 'Whatever can have its description fixed and its quantity known, salam is valid in it.' This includes measurable, weighable, length-measurable items.
The conditions are designed to prevent uncertainty (gharar).
2
Identify exclusions
Items that cannot be precisely described (animals, jewels, pearls) are invalid. Also, currencies (dirhams/dinars) are excluded because they are prices, not commodities.
The author says 'al-muthamman' (fungible) to exclude dirhams/dinars.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on wording
Some Hanafis (Zufar, 'Isa ibn Aban) hold that salam cannot be concluded with the word 'bay'' ('sale'). The preferred view (al-asahh) is that it can, as transmitted from al-Hasan.
The text says 'al-asahh' (the more correct) is that it is concluded with the word 'sale'.
4
Consider the scenario when conditions are not met
If a salam contract is invalid due to missing conditions, it may be considered a regular sale with deferred payment, but this is disputed among Hanafis.
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on this point.

Chapter on Salam: i.e., explaining its rulings. Whatever can have its description fixed and its quantity known, salam is valid in it; and what cannot, then not. So it is valid in measurable items, everything whose description is known, and weighable items that are fungible (al-muthamman), like honey and oil. Chapter on Salam: (with fatha on the lam) literally means haste, and legally it is a sale of a thing on the basis that it is a debt on the seller with valid conditions. Know that selling a specific item for a debt is a resolve ('azima), and selling a debt for a specific item is a concession (rukhsa). When he finished explaining the first, he started on the second, which is salam. This type of sale is distinguished by this name because of its specific ruling that indicates its literal meaning: the acceleration of one of the two compensations before the presence of the sold item. The sold item is called muslam fih, the price is ra's al-mal, the seller is muslam ilayh, and the buyer is rabb al-salam. It is legislated by the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the ummah. Ibn Abbas said: 'I bear witness that Allah permitted deferred salam.' And he recited the verse: 'O you who have believed, when you contract a debt for a specified term, write it down' (2:282). And we have narrated that he (the Prophet) forbade selling what is not with a person, but he granted a concession for salam. The reason, and analogy (qiyas) would prohibit its permissibility because the muslam fih is a sold item and it is non-existent, and selling an existing item that is not owned, or owned but not in possession for delivery, is not permissible, so selling a non-existent item is even more so. But we abandoned analogy for what we mentioned. And it is synonymous with 'salaf' linguistically, and from it are derived 'salam', 'islam', 'salaf', 'islaf'. Legally, it is a contract that establishes ownership of the price immediately and of the priced item (muslam fih) deferred. Some say: it is taking an immediate thing for a deferred thing. And it is not concluded with the word 'sale' (bay'). This is the view of Zufar, 'Isa ibn Aban, and al-Shafi'i in one opinion. In the narration of al-Hasan: it is concluded, and this is the more correct (asahh). Its pillars are offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). It is concluded with the word 'salam' and upon that the narrations agree. The more correct view is that it is also concluded with the word 'sale'. Its legislation is due to severe need. Its ruling: ownership of the price is established for the muslam ilayh, and for the rabb al-salam, ownership of the muslam fih as a debt in the dhimmah; as for the specific item (ayn), it is not established except by taking possession (qabd). (from 'Ayni, Fath). And knowing its quantity: like measurable, weighable, and length-measurable items. In some copies: 'knowing its measure.' (from 'Ayni) Salam is valid in it because it does not lead to dispute after description is fixed and quantity known. And the Prophet said: 'Whoever among you advances salam in dates, let him advance salam in known measure and known weight to a known term.' Reported by Bukhari and Muslim. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/70). 'And what cannot, then not': i.e., every thing in which description and quantity cannot be fixed, salam is not valid in it because it leads to dispute, like animals, jewels, and pearls. (from Ta', 'Ayni). 'al-muthamman' (fungible): and he restricted it with his saying 'al-muthamman' to exclude dirhams and dinars, for they are weighable but they are not fungible (muthamman) but rather are prices (athman) by nature, so salam is not permissible in them. If the capital (ra's al-mal) is also a price, the contract is void completely. And if it is other than the price, like a cloth for ten dirhams, it is not valid as salam by agreement. And is it concluded as a sale with a deferred price? Abu Bakr al-A'mash said: yes. 'Isa ibn Aban said: no, and this is the more correct, because the correction of the contract is only in the place that necessitated... (incomplete in original).. agreement.

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Salam (advance sale) in the book of sales. It defines salam linguistically (haste) and legally (a sale where the price is paid immediately for a specific described good to be delivered later). The author notes that salam is a concession (rukhsa) from the general prohibition of selling what one does not possess, which is a strict ruling ('azima). The proof of its legitimacy is from the Quran (2:282) and the hadith of the Prophet allowing salam with known measure and weight. The text explains that qiyas would prohibit salam because the sold item is non-existent at the time of contract, but this qiyas is set aside by the revealed texts (istihsan). The conditions for validity are that the item must be describable and quantifiable (like measurable, weighable, or length-measurable items). Items that cannot be precisely described (like jewels) are excluded. The text also discusses the dispute over whether salam can be concluded using the word 'sale' (bay')—the preferred view is that it can, though some early Hanafis (Zufar, 'Isa ibn Aban) and al-Shafi'i say no. Additionally, salam is not valid in currencies (dinars and dirhams) because they are prices themselves, not commodities. Finally, if a salam contract is formed but the conditions are not met, it may be considered a regular sale with deferred payment, though there is disagreement on this.

Key Terms
مسلم فيه (muslam fih): The item to be delivered in the future under a salam contract.
مثمن (muthamman): Fungible commodity that is sold for a price.
ثمن (thaman): Price; in salam, the price is called ra's al-mal.

Salam requires the subject to be precisely described and quantifiable; currencies and non-describable items are excluded; the contract can be concluded with the word 'sale' according to the preferred view.

Definition and Legitimacy of Salam (Forward Sale)
Opening of Salam chapter: definition, legitimacy, and wording dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define salam, identify its scriptural basis, and explain why the Hanafi school permits it despite the general prohibition of selling what is not possessed.

Core Idea
Salam is a sale of a deferred debt — permitted as a concession despite the qiyas prohibition against selling the nonexistent.

Salam is a forward sale where the price is paid immediately and the goods are delivered later. This lesson explains its definition, the scriptural evidence that overrides the normal rule, and the intra-Hanafi debate about whether it can be concluded with the word 'sale'.

Guided Path
1
Parse the linguistic and legal definitions of salam
Start with the linguistic meaning (haste) and then the legal definition: selling something as a deferred debt against immediate payment with conditions.
2
Identify the three sources of legitimacy
Note the Quranic verse (2:282) cited by Ibn Abbas, the Prophetic concession despite the general prohibition, and the consensus of the Ummah.
The Quranic dalil is qati al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah.
3
Understand the qiyas objection and its override
Qiyas would prohibit salam because the sold item is nonexistent. The Hanafi school abandons qiyas due to the specific textual evidence (istihsan).
This is a clear example of istihsan overriding qiyas.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on using the word 'sale'
Some scholars (Zufar, Isa ibn Aban, al-Shafi'i) restrict salam to the word 'salam'; the most authentic Hanafi view allows it with 'sale' as well.
The text states 'wa huwa al-asahh' for al-Hasan's narration.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position and its practical effect
The relied-upon position is that salam is valid with the word 'sale', providing flexibility in contracts.
This affects how contracts are enforced in case of wording disputes.

باب السلم: [بالتحريك لغة: هو الاستعجال، وشرعا هو بيع الشيء على أن يكون ديناً على البائع بالشرائط المعتبرة اعلم أن بيع العين بالدين عزيمة، وبيع الدين بالعين رخصة، فلما فرغ من بيان الأول شرع في الثاني، وهو السلم واختص هذا النوع من البيع هذا الاسم؛ لاختصاصه بحكم يدل على معناه اللغوي، وهو تعجيل أحد البدلين قبل حضور المبيع، فالمبيع يسمى مسلماً فيه، والثمن رأس المال والبائع مسلماً إليه، والمشتري رب السلم، وهو مشروع بالكتاب والسنة وإجماع الأمة قال ابن عباس هما: أشهد أن الله أحل السلم المؤجل، وتلا قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِذَا تَدَايَنْتُمْ بِدَيْنِ إِلَى أَجَلٍ مُسَمّى فَاكْتُبُوهُ ﴾ (البقرة: ۲۸۲)، وقد روينا أنه عليها فهي عن بيع ما ليس عند الإنسان، ورخص في السلم. والقياس يأبى جوازه؛ لأن المسلم فيه مبيع، وهو معدوم، وبيع موجود غير مملوك، أو مملوك غير مقدور التسليم لا يجوز، فبيع المعدوم أولى، ولكن تركناه بما ذكرنا، وهو بمعنى السلف لغة، وسمي منه سلماً وإسلاماً وسلفاً وإسلافاً، وشرعاً: هو عقد يثبت الملك في الثمن عاجلاً وفي المثمن آجلاً، وقيل: هو أخذ عاجل بآجل، ولا ينعقد بلفظ البيع، وبه قال زفر وعيسى بن أبان والشافعي في قول، وفي رواية الحسن: ينعقد، وهو الأصح، وركنه: الإيجاب والقبول، وينعقد بلفظ السلم وعليه اتفاق الروايات والأصح أنه ينعقد بلفظ البيع أيضاً، مشروعيته شدة الحاجة وحكمه ثبوت الملك للمسلم إليه في الثمن، ولرب السلم في المسلم فيه الدين في الذمة، أما في العين فلا يثبت إلا بقبضه.
Chapter of Salam: [Salam linguistically means haste. Legally, it is selling something as a debt upon the seller with the required conditions. Know that selling a specific item for a debt is a determined rule, and selling a debt for a specific item is a concession. When he finished explaining the first, he began the second, which is Salam. This type of sale is distinguished by this name due to its specific ruling that indicates its linguistic meaning: advancing one of the two countervalues before the presence of the sold item. The sold item is called muslam fihi, the price is ra's al-mal, the seller is muslam ilayhi, and the buyer is rabb al-salam. It is legislated by the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the Ummah. Ibn Abbas said: 'I bear witness that Allah permitted deferred Salam,' and he recited the verse: 'O you who have believed, when you contract a debt for a specified term, write it down' (2:282). It has been narrated that he (peace be upon him) prohibited selling what is not in one's possession, but granted a concession for Salam. Analogy (qiyas) prohibits its permissibility because the muslam fihi is a sold item that is nonexistent, and selling an existing item that is not owned, or owned but not capable of delivery, is not permitted, so selling a nonexistent item is even more so. But we abandoned qiyas due to what we mentioned. It is synonymous with salaf linguistically, and it is called salam, islam, salaf, and islaf. Legally, it is a contract that establishes ownership of the price immediately and of the priced item at a future date. It is said it is taking an advance for a deferred item. It is not concluded with the word 'sale'; this is the view of Zufar, Isa ibn Aban, and al-Shafi'i in one narration. In the narration of al-Hasan, it is concluded, which is the most authentic. Its pillar is offer and acceptance. It is concluded with the word salam by agreement of the narrations, and the most authentic view is that it is also concluded with the word 'sale'. Its legislation is due to dire need, and its ruling is that ownership of the price is established for the muslam ilayhi, and for rabb al-salam, ownership of the muslam fihi is established as a debt in the dhimma; as for the specific item, it is not established except by taking possession.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Salam (forward sale) in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It first gives the linguistic and legal definitions: linguistically, salam means haste; legally, it is a sale where the sold item is a deferred debt on the seller, with all required conditions. The author notes that selling a specific item (ayn) for a debt (dayn) is the general rule (azima), while selling a debt for a specific item is a concession (rukhsa). After explaining the general sale, he now turns to this special type. The name 'salam' reflects its linguistic meaning: one of the two countervalues (the price) is given immediately before the presence of the sold item. The terms are explained: the sold item is muslam fihi, the price is ra's al-mal, the seller is muslam ilayhi, and the buyer is rabb al-salam. The legitimacy is established by three proofs: Quran (verse 2:282, which Ibn Abbas cited as permission for deferred salam), Sunnah (the Prophet prohibited selling what is not possessed but granted a concession for salam), and Ijma (consensus). Then the text raises a qiyas objection: since the muslam fihi is nonexistent at the time of contract, and selling a nonexistent item is normally invalid, salam should be invalid. However, the Hanafi school overrides this qiyas with the specific textual evidence (the Quran and hadith concession). The passage then gives additional names: salam, islam, salaf, islaf. Legally, it is a contract that establishes immediate ownership of the price and deferred ownership of the priced item in the dhimma. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether it can be concluded with the word 'sale' (lafz al-bay'): Zufar, Isa ibn Aban, and al-Shafi'i say no; al-Hasan ibn Ziyad reports that it is concluded, and this is considered the most authentic (asahh) view. The pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance. The ruling (hukm) is that ownership of the price transfers immediately to the seller, and the buyer owns the described item as a debt in the seller's dhimma; ownership of a specific item (ayn) only occurs upon delivery.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A sale where the price is paid immediately and the sold item is delivered later as a debt on the seller.
مسلم فيه (muslam fihi): The sold item in a salam contract, which is a deferred debt in the seller's dhimma.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The immediate payment given by the buyer in a salam contract; the capital.
رب السلم (rabb al-salam): The buyer in a salam contract, who pays the price and receives the described item later.

Key Takeaway
Salam is a legally permissible forward sale, allowed by specific textual evidence despite the general prohibition, and the Hanafi school permits its conclusion with the word 'sale'.

The Foundational Condition for Salam: Precision in Description and Measurement
States the general rule for valid salam and explores boundary cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for valid salam, identify the proof from hadith, and explain why dirhams and dinars are excluded from salam.

Core Idea
Salam requires precise description and measurement of the commodity; dirhams and dinars are prices, not commodities, so salam in them is invalid.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lays down the fundamental rule for salam: the item must be capable of precise description and known quantity. It cites the famous hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and then explores boundary cases, including the exclusion of dirhams and dinars and the intra-school debate on reinterpreting a failed salam contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general condition
Read the opening statement: 'Whatever can have its description precisely defined and its quantity known, salam in it is valid.' Pinpoint the two required elements: ضبط الصفة (precise description of quality) and معرفة القدر (knowledge of quantity).
These are cumulative—both must be present.
2
Locate the proof
Find the hadith cited: "Whoever among you makes salam in dates, let him make salam in a known measure and a known weight up to a known term." Understand that this hadith is the primary dalil, and its explicit command for known measure and weight indicates that precision is a condition.
The hadith is narrated by Bukhari and Muslim, so its thubut is qati' (certain).
3
List the categories of valid salam items
The passage specifies two categories: makil (things measured by volume) and mawzun muthamman (weighed commodities like honey and oil). Note that both must be muthamman (commodities), not athman (prices).
The term 'muthamman' restricts the category.
4
Analyze the exclusion of dirhams and dinars
The text explicitly excludes dirhams and dinars because they are athman (prices) by nature, not muthman. Understand the Hanafi reasoning: allowing salam in dirhams would lead to a contract where both the capital and the subject are prices, causing confusion (gharar). Compare with the Maliki view that permits it.
This is a key point of ikhtilaf.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on reinterpretation
If a salam contract fails (e.g., attempting salam in dirhams), can it be reinterpreted as a regular sale with deferred payment? The passage presents two views: Abu Bakr al-A'mash says yes, Isa ibn Aban says no. The text declares Isa ibn Aban's view as 'al-asahh' (most authentic). Understand the reasoning: the contract was not intended as a sale of the underlying commodity (e.g., cloth), so it cannot be corrected in a different direction.
This applies the principle that contracts are interpreted based on the parties' intent.

ما أمكن ضبط صفته ومعرفة قدره صح السّلمُ فيه، وما لا فلا، فيصح في المكيل كل شيء أدرك وصفه والموزون المثمن. كالعسل والزيت. ما أمكن ضبط صفته ومعرفة قدره صح السلم فيه: لأنه لا يفضي إلى المنازعة بعد ضبط الوصف ومعرفة القدر، وقد قال عليه: "من أسلم منكم في تمر، فليسلم في كيل معلوم ووزن معلوم إلى أجل معلوم"، رواه البخاري ومسلم. وما لا فلا [ أي كل شيء لا يمكن فيه ضبط الصفة ومعرفة القدر.(عيني) فلا يصح السلم فيه؛ لأنه يفضي إلى المنازعة كالحيوان والجواهر واللآلي. (ط، عيني المثمن وقيد بقوله: "المثمن" احترازاً عن الدراهم والدنانير، فإنهما موزونة، ولكنهما غير مثمنة بل أثمان خلقةً، فلا يجوز السلم فيهما، فإنه إذا كان رأس المال ثمناً أيضاً كان العقد باطلاً وإن كان غير الثمن كثوب في عشرة دراهم لم يصح سلماً اتفاقاً، وهل ينعقد بيعاً بثمن مؤجل؟ قال أبو بكر الأعمش: ينعقد، وقال عيسى بن أبان: لا ينعقد، وهو الأصح؛ لأن تصحيح العقد إنما يكون في المحل الذي أوجب المتعاقدان البيع فيه لا في غيره، وهما لم يوجباه إلا في الدراهم، ولا يمكن التصحيح باعتبارها بل باعتبار الثوب، ولم يوجباه فيه، فكان في غير محله، ولو أسلم في المكيل وزناً كما إذا أسلم في البر والشعير بالميزان، روى الطحاوي عن أصحابنا: أنه يجوز ، وروى الحسن أنه لا يجوز، وعند مالك: يجوز السلم في الدراهم والدنانير.
Whatever can have its description precisely defined and its quantity known, salam in it is valid; otherwise not. So it is valid in makil (things measured by volume), everything whose description is known, and in mawzun al-muthamman (things weighed that are commodities), like honey and oil. 'Whatever can have its description precisely defined and its quantity known, salam in it is valid' because it does not lead to dispute after defining the description and knowing the quantity. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever among you makes salam in dates, let him make salam in a known measure and a known weight up to a known term,' narrated by Bukhari and Muslim. 'Otherwise not' means for anything in which description cannot be defined and quantity known, salam in it is not valid, because it leads to dispute, like animals, jewels, and pearls. 'Al-muthamman' is a restriction (qayd) to exclude dirhams and dinars, for they are weighed (mawzun) but they are not commodities (muthamman); rather they are by nature prices (athman). So salam in them is not valid. If the capital is also a price (thaman), the contract is invalid. If it is other than a price, like a cloth for ten dirhams, it is not valid as salam by agreement. Then is it valid as a sale with deferred price? Abu Bakr al-A'mash said: it is valid; Isa ibn Aban said: it is not valid, which is the most authentic, because correcting the contract can only be in the place where the two contractors intended the sale, not elsewhere, and they only intended it in the dirhams, and correction cannot be by considering them but by considering the cloth, which they did not intend, so it is out of place. And if one makes salam in a makil by weight, like salam in wheat or barley by scale, al-Tahawi narrated from our companions that it is valid, and al-Hasan narrated that it is not valid. Malik said: salam in dirhams and dinars is valid.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage lays down the fundamental condition for the validity of salam: the item sold must be capable of precise description and measurement. The two key elements are: (1) ضبط الصفة (precise description of quality) and (2) معرفة القدر (knowledge of quantity). This is based on the Prophetic hadith recorded by Bukhari and Muslim: 'Whoever makes salam dates, let it be in known measure, known weight, and known term.' The reason is to prevent future disputes (munaza'a). Valid salam items include: (a) makil (things measured by volume, like grains), (b) mawzun al-muthamman (weighed commodities, such as honey and oil). The term al-muthamman (commodity) is expressly restricted to exclude dirhams and dinars, even though they are also weighed. Dirhams and dinars are athman (prices) by nature, not commodities; thus salam in them is invalid. If someone tries to make salam with a cloth as the item and dinars as the price, it is invalid as salam by consensus. However, there is a intra-Hanafi dispute whether such a contract can be reinterpreted as a regular sale with deferred payment (بثمن مؤجل). Abu Bakr al-A'mash accepts it; Isa ibn Aban rejects it, and the latter is considered more correct (al-asahh). Also, if one makes salam in a makil item using weight (e.g., wheat by weight rather than volume), there are two narrations: Tahawi reports permissibility from the Hanafi imams; al-Hasan reports prohibition. Malik's school permits salam in dirhams and dinars, which contrasts with the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
مكيل (makil): Things measured by volume (e.g., grains, liquids) that are suitable for salam.
موزون مثمن (mawzun muthamman): Weighed items that are commodities (e.g., honey, oil), as opposed to dirhams and dinars which are prices.
مثمن (muthamman): A commodity that is sold, as opposed to a price (thaman). In salam, the sold item must be a muthamman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school exclude dirhams and dinars from valid salam contracts?
A) Because they are not measurable by weight
B) Because they function as prices (athman), not commodities (muthman)
C) Because the hadith explicitly forbids salam in dirhams
D) Because they cannot be described precisely

Key Takeaway
The validity of salam hinges on the subject being precisely describable and measurable, and dirhams and dinars are excluded because they are prices, not commodities.

Valid Items for Salam: Uniform Countables, Non-Uniforms, and Specific Cases
Details valid salam items and intra-Hanafi disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid salam in uniform and non-uniform countable items, identify the Hanafi positions on eggs, fals, bricks, and cloth, and evaluate the intra-madhhab disputes with Zufar, Abu Hanifa, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Salam is valid in uniform countable items by number or measure; non-uniform items require precise specification to be treated as uniform.

Salam (advance payment sale) requires precise description of the subject matter to avoid uncertainty. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, with commentary from al-Ayni and al-Fath, details which types of items qualify: uniform countable items (like walnuts and eggs), non-uniform items that can be specified (like bricks with a known mold), and items measured by length (like unsewn cloth). It also addresses intra-Hanafi disputes: Zufar's objection to counting, Abu Hanifa's caution on ostrich eggs, and Muhammad's ruling on fals. Understanding these distinctions is essential for structuring valid salam contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of salam items
Classify items into uniform countable (adadi mutaqarib), non-uniform countable (adadi mutafawit), and dhari'i (measured by length). For each, note the default ruling.
The text puts walnuts, eggs, fals, bricks under uniform; watermelons, pearls under non-uniform; cloth under dhari'i.
2
Understand the Hanafi response to Zufar's objection
Zufar argued that uniform countable items still have variation, making salam invalid by number. The Hanafi response: customary practice disregards minor variation, so counting is valid.
The text says 'kuwnuhu ma'dūdan bi-istilāḥi-himā fa-jāza ihdāruhu' meaning since it is considered countable by their convention, the variation is overlooked.
3
Analyze non-uniform countable items and conditions for validity
Salam is invalid in non-uniform items like pearls or leather unless they are specified with exact length, width, thickness, and all salam conditions, thereby treating them as uniform.
The text quotes Abu Yusuf's definition of non-uniform and the exception clause.
4
Examine specific items: eggs, fals, bricks, and cloth
For eggs: salam is valid absolutely (including ostrich eggs per majority), but Abu Hanifa's narration prohibits ostrich eggs. For fals: preferred view permits salam; Muhammad prohibits it as a price. For bricks: mold (malban) must be known with dimensions. For cloth: must be unsewn.
The text discusses each with intra-madhhab disagreements and final tarjih.
5
Identify the mufta bihi positions
The relied-upon rulings: eggs permitted generally; fals permitted (as per al-Jami' al-Saghir), bricks with specified mold, unsewn cloth. These are from Zahir al-Riwayah.
The text calls the fals view 'al-asahh' (most correct) and notes no disagreement in al-Jami' al-Saghir.

(عيني) والبيض: مطلقاً سواء كان بيض نعامة أو غيره، وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة: أن السلم لا يصح في بيض النعامة؛ لتفاوت آحاده في القيمة، وقيل: إن أسلم فيه للأكل جاز، وإلا فلا. (عيني، فتح) والفلس: أي ويصح السلم في الفلس؛ لإمكان ضبطه بالعدد، وعند محمد: لا يصح السلم فيه؛ لأنه ثمن ما دام يروج، ولهذا لا يجوز بيع الفلس بالفلسين عنده، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، إلا أن ظاهر الرواية عن محمد كقولهما، وهو الأصح، ولهذا لم يحك في الجامع الصغير" خلافاً. (عيني، فتح) واللبن: بكسر اللام والباء، وهو الطوب النيء. الآجر : بضم الجيم وتشديد الراء وهو الطوب المحرق. (ط، عيني) ملبن معلوم لأن آحاده لا تختلف اختلافاً يفضي إلى المنازعة بعد ذكر الآلة. "والملين" بكسر الميم: قالب الطين. وفي "القاموس : الملبن كمنبر : قالب اللبن واللبن كإبل، ويجوز إسكان الموحدة فيصير مثل حمل، ويحتمل أن يكون الملبن اسما لما يضرب منه اللبن، وإنما يصير الملبن معلوماً إذا ذكر طوله وعرضه وسمكه، وشرط في "الخلاصة" ذكر الموضع الذي يعمل منه اللبن، وإذا لم يتعين الملبن لا يجوز السلم؛ لأنه يفضي إلى المنازعة. (عيني، فتح) والذرعي: أي ويصح السلم في الشيء الذي يذرع كالثوب والبساط والحصير إلحاقاً لها بالمكيل والموزون بجامع الحاجة، وأراد بالثوب غير المخيط، أما المخيط، فلا يجوز السلم فيه كالفراء والقلانس والخفاف.
And the countable items that are uniform (adadi mutaqarib), like walnuts, eggs, fals (small coin), unbaked brick (labin), and baked brick (ajur) if a known mold (malban) is specified. And salam is valid in countable items by number. And the measured by length (dhari'i) like cloth. 'And the countable items that are uniform': meaning salam is valid in uniform countable items by number because the quantity is known sometimes by number and sometimes by measurement (kayl), so precision is possible with both; thus it is permissible either by number or by measurement. Zufar said: it is not valid by number because of the variation in its units. We reply: its being counted in their terminology is sufficient, so the variation is disregarded; it is disregarded customarily. And it is narrated from him (Zufar) that it is not valid by measurement. The restriction 'uniform' is because salam is not valid in non-uniform countable items like watermelon and pomegranates, except according to al-Shafi'i: it is valid absolutely. As for non-uniform countable items, its interpretation transmitted from Abu Yusuf is: what varies in its units - salam is not valid in it, like pearls, jewels, leather, skins, wood, heads, trotters, quince, and similar items. Unless from the category of skins, leather, and wood, one specifies a known item with known length, known width, and known thickness, and brings all the conditions of salam, then it is annexed to the uniform and is valid. 'Like walnuts': because they are of known quantity, precisely described, and capable of delivery. 'Eggs': absolutely, whether ostrich eggs or others. Al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that salam is not valid in ostrich eggs due to variation in value. It is said: if salam in them is for eating, it is permissible; otherwise not. 'Fals': salam in fals is valid because it can be precisely defined by number. According to Muhammad, salam in fals is not valid because it is a price (thaman) as long as it circulates; therefore, selling a fals for two fals is not permissible according to him. Malik and Ahmad also held this view. However, the apparent narration from Muhammad is like their view, which is the most authentic; therefore, no disagreement is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir. 'Labin' (with kasra on lam and ba): it is unbaked brick. 'Ajur' (with damma on jeem and tashdid on ra): it is baked brick. 'Malban known': because its units do not vary in a way that leads to dispute after specifying the mold. 'Al-malban' (with kasra on meem): the clay mold. In al-Qamus: al-malban (like minbar) is the mold for bricks; al-labin (like ibil) is bricks; it may also be with sukun on the ba (like haml). And it is possible that al-malban is the name for the tool from which bricks are made. The malban becomes known only when its length, width, and thickness are mentioned. In al-Khulasah, it is conditioned to mention the place from which the brick is made. If the malban is not specified, salam is not valid because it leads to dispute. 'And the measured by length (dhari'i)': meaning salam is valid in that which is measured by length, like cloth, rug, mat, analogized to makil and mawzun due to common need. By 'cloth' he means unsewn cloth; as for sewn items, salam is not valid in them, like furs, caps, and socks.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage details additional types of items in which salam is valid, beyond the earlier categories. The first category is 'adadi mutaqarib' (countable items that are uniform in size). Examples are walnuts, eggs, fals (a small copper coin), and bricks (labin and ajur) provided the mold is specified. Zufar objected that counting uniform items still has variation, but the Hanafi response is that customary practice disregards minor variation. The text also discusses non-uniform countable items (adadi mutafawit) like watermelons, pomegranates, pearls, and skins. The general rule is that salam is invalid in such items unless they are specified with precise dimensions and all salam conditions are met, in which case they become analogous to uniform items. For eggs, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Al-Hasan narrates that Abu Hanifa prohibited salam in ostrich eggs due to value variation, while the general view allows all eggs. The fals is a special case: Imam Muhammad prohibits salam in it because it functions as a price (thaman) while circulating, analogous to dirhams. However, the preferred view in the school permits it, as stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir. For bricks (labin and ajur), the key is specifying the mold (malban) with dimensions and sometimes the place of production to avoid dispute. The last category is dhari'i: items measured by length, like unsewn cloth, rugs, mats. This is analogized to makil and mawzun due to the need for such items. Sewn items like caps and socks are excluded because their shape and size cannot be precisely standardized.

Key Terms
العددي المتقارب (al-adadi al-mutaqarib): Countable items whose individual units are nearly identical in size and value, e.g., walnuts, eggs.
العددي المتفاوت (al-adadi al-mutafawit): Countable items whose units vary significantly, e.g., watermelons, pearls.
الفلس (al-fals): A small copper coin used as currency (pl. fulus).
الذرعي (al-dhari'i): Items measured by length (dhira'), e.g., cloth, rugs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the most authentic Hanafi view, is salam valid in fals (copper coin)?
A) No, because it is a price
B) Yes, because it can be precisely counted
C) Only if it is mixed with other items
D) Only for eating

Key Takeaway
Salam is valid in uniform countable items and precisely described non-uniform items; intra-madhhab disputes are resolved by giving weight to the apparent narration and customary practice.

Salam in Similar Countable Goods: Rulings and Disputes
Explains rulings for salam in similar countable goods with examples and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on salam in similar countable goods and distinguish between al-addadi al-mutaqarib and al-addadi al-mutafawit.

Core Idea
Salam is valid in similar countable goods by count; it is invalid in variable goods without specification.

This lesson expands on the ruling (hukm) for salam (forward sale) in goods that are individually similar (al-addadi al-mutaqarib) and those that are variable (al-addadi al-mutafawit). The author of Kanz al-Daqaiq presents the general rule with examples, and the commentaries (from al-‘Ayni and al-Fath) clarify the conditions and intra-Hanafi disputes. By understanding this, you will be able to correctly determine the validity of salam contracts in everyday transactions involving countable items like eggs, nuts, and bricks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is salam (forward sale) in goods that are countable and similar in unit size. The ruling: it is valid (sahih) by count or by measure.
The passage begins with 'walddadi al-mutaqarib' to define the category.
2
Classify the type of good
Distinguish between al-addadi al-mutaqarib (similar units like walnuts) and al-addadi al-mutafawit (variable units like melons). The ruling depends on this classification.
The key is whether individual units vary significantly in size/value, leading to potential dispute.
3
Examine the examples and conditions
Walk through each example: walnuts, eggs, fals, unbaked brick (labin), baked brick (ajur). Note the condition for bricks: the mold (malbin) must be known.
For eggs: generally valid, but there is an exception for ostrich eggs (value variation).
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on fals (coin)
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold salam in fals is valid by count; Muhammad says it is invalid because it is a price. The relied-upon view is validity.
The commentary notes that the correct (asahh) view is from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i position on variable goods
The Hanafi school requires detailed specification for variable goods (e.g., length, thickness). Al-Shafi'i considers salam absolute. The thamarah al-khilaf is whether a general description suffices.
The Hanafi response: variability leads to dispute even after description, unlike human-manufactured goods.

(عيني، فتح) والفلس: أي ويصح السلم في الفلس؛ لإمكان ضبطه بالعدد، وعند محمد: لا يصح السلم فيه؛ لأنه ثمن ما دام يروج، ولهذا لا يجوز بيع الفلس بالفلسين عنده، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، إلا أن ظاهر الرواية عن محمد كقولهما، وهو الأصح، ولهذا لم يحك في الجامع الصغير" خلافاً. (عيني، فتح) واللبن: بكسر اللام والباء، وهو الطوب النيء. الآجر : بضم الجيم وتشديد الراء وهو الطوب المحرق. (ط، عيني) ملبن معلوم لأن آحاده لا تختلف اختلافاً يفضي إلى المنازعة بعد ذكر الآلة. "والملين" بكسر الميم: قالب الطين. وفي "القاموس : الملبن كمنبر : قالب اللبن واللبن كإبل، ويجوز إسكان الموحدة فيصير مثل حمل، ويحتمل أن يكون الملبن اسما لما يضرب منه اللبن، وإنما يصير الملبن معلوماً إذا ذكر طوله وعرضه وسمكه، وشرط في "الخلاصة" ذكر الموضع الذي يعمل منه اللبن، وإذا لم يتعين الملبن لا يجوز السلم؛ لأنه يفضي إلى المنازعة. (عيني، فتح)
Book of Sales 335: Chapter of Salam. And similar countable (goods) like walnuts, eggs, the false coin, unbaked brick, and baked brick if a known mold is named, and it is valid in countable (goods) by count, and the length-measured like cloth. [Commentary:]. . .
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rules of salam (forward sale) for goods that are similar in size (al-addadi al-mutaqarib). The author states that salam is valid in such goods by count, because their quantity can be known by counting or by measuring, giving examples like walnuts, eggs, fals (coin), unbaked bricks, and baked bricks, provided the mold (malbin) for bricks is specified. The commentary then discusses a dispute: Zufar argues it is not valid by count due to individual variation, but the response is that since the parties treat them as countable, the variation is disregarded. The passage also discusses salam in goods that are variable (al-addadi al-mutafawit) like melons and pomegranates, which is not valid unless specification is given, except according to al-Shafi'i who allows it absolutely. The Hanafi view is that such goods require detailed specification of type, length, thickness, etc. The text then goes through each example: walnuts are valid because known and deliverable; eggs are valid generally, but there is a narration from Abu Hanifa that ostrich eggs are not valid due to value variation; fals is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad says it is invalid because it is a price as long as it circulates; the relied-upon view is that it is valid as per the two shaykhs. Unbaked and baked bricks are valid if the mold is specified (dimensions and source of clay). The passage also includes a brief digression on salam in goods normally sold by measure but given by weight, and vice versa, with a dispute between Tahawi (valid) and Hasan (invalid). Overall, the passage establishes that the key to validity in salam is the ability to know the subject matter precisely to avoid future dispute.

Key Terms
العددي المتقارب (al-addadi al-mutaqarib): Countable goods whose individual units are roughly similar in size and value, such as walnuts and eggs.
العددي المتفاوت (al-addadi al-mutafawit): Countable goods whose individual units vary significantly in size and value, such as melons and pomegranates.
الملبن (al-malbin): The mold used for making bricks (or the form for clay bricks). In salam for bricks, the mold must be specified (dimensions).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is salam in eggs valid by count?
A) Yes, absolutely
B) No, it is not valid
C) It is valid only if specified by weight
D) It is valid except for ostrich eggs

Key Takeaway
Salam in similar countable goods is valid by count; in variable goods, specification is essential.
